 CHAPTER I 

 the common FEVER 


 a warm summer evening , with a sultry haze brood over the level landscape , and a Sabbath stillness upon all thing in the village of Lidford , Midlandshire . in the remoter corner of the old gothic church the shadow be begin to gather , as the sermon draw near its close ; but in the centre aisle and about the pulpit there be broad daylight still shin - in from the wide western window , across the low half of which there be tall figure of the evangelist in old stain glass . 

 there be no chorister at Lidford , and the evening service be conduct in rather a drowsy way ; but there be a solemn air of repose about the gray old church that should be conducive to tranquil thought and pious meditation . simple and earnest have be the word of the sermon , simple and earnest seem the countenance of the congregation , look reverently upwards at the face of their pastor ; and one might fancy , contemplate that grand old church , so much too spacious for the need of the little flock gather there to - night , that Lidford be a forgotten , half - desert corner of this earth , in which a man , tired of the press and turmoil of the world , might find an almost monastic solitude and calm . 

 so think a gentleman in the Squire 's pew a good - look man of about thirty , who be finish his first Sunday at Lidford by devout attendance at evening service . he have be think a good deal about this quiet country life during the service , wonder whether it be not the good life a man could live , after all , and think it all the sweet because of his own experience , which have lie chiefly in city . 

 he be a certain Mr. Gilbert Fenton , an australian merchant , and be on a visit to his sister , who have marry the principal landowner in Lidford , Martin Lister a man whose father have be call " the squire . " the lady sit opposite her brother in the wide old family pew to - night a handsome - look matron , with a little rosy - cheeked damsel sit by her side a damsel with flow auburn hair , tiny hat and feather , and bright scarlet stocking , look very much as if she have walk out of a picture by Mr. Millais .

 it be a face that a man could scarcely look upon once without find his glance wander back to it afterwards ; not quite a perfect face , but a very bright and win one . large gray eye , with a wonderful light in they , under dark lash and dark brow ; a complexion that have a dusky pallor , a delicate semi - transparent olive - tint that one seldom see out of a spanish picture ; a sweet rosy mouth , and a piquant little nose of no particular order , make up the catalogue of this young lady 's charm . but in a face worth look at there be always a something that can not be put into word ; and the bright and good attribute of this face be quite beyond translation . it be a face one might almost call ' splendid " there be such a light and glory about it at some moment . Gilbert Fenton think so to - night , as he see it in the full radiance of the western sunlight , the lip part as the girl sing , the clear gray eye look upward . 

 she be not alone : a portly genial - look old man stand by her side , and accompany she to the church - porch when the hymn be over . here they both linger a moment to shake hand with Mrs. Lister , very much to Gilbert Fenton 's satisfaction . they walk along the churchyard - path together , and Gilbert give his sister 's arm a little tug , which mean , " introduce I . " 

 " my brother Mr. Fenton , Captain Sedgewick , Miss Nowell . " 

 the Captain shake hand with Gilbert . " delighted to know you , Mr. Fenton ; delight to know any one belong to Mrs. Lister . you be go to stop down here for some time , I hope . " 

 " I fear not for very long , Captain Sedgewick . I be a business man , you see , and can not   afford to take a long holiday from the City . " 

 Mrs. Lister laugh . " my brother be utterly devoted to commercial pursuit , " she say ; " I think he believe every hour waste that he spend out of his counting - house . "

 " you would very soon be tired of your dreamy idleness , " answer his sister , " and sigh for your office and your club . " 

 " the country suit old people , who have play their part in life , and make an end of it , " say the Captain . " it suit my little girl here very well , too , " he add , with a fond glance at his companion ; ' she have her bird and her flower , and her book and music ; and I do not   think she ever sigh for anything gayer than Lidford . " 

 " never , uncle George , " say the girl , slip her hand through his arm . and Gilbert Fenton see that those two be very fond of each other . 

 they come to the end of a shady wind lane at this moment , and Captain Sedgewick and Miss Nowell wish Mrs. Lister and her brother good - evening , and go away down the lane arm - in - arm . 

 " what a lovely girl she be ! " say Gilbert , when they be go . 

 " Lovely be rather a strong word , Gilbert , " Mrs. Lister answer coldly ; ' she be certainly pretty , but I hope you be not go to lose your heart in that direction . " 

 " there be no fear of that . a man may admire a girl 's face without be in any danger of lose his heart . but why not in that direction , Belle ? be there any special objection to the lady ? " 

 " only that she be a nobody , without either money or position and I think you ought to have both when you marry . "

I know that since I be a boy , and get over my schoolboy love affair , I have never see the woman who could touch my heart . I have meet plenty of pretty woman , and plenty of brilliant woman , of course , in society ; and have admire they , and there an end . I have never see a woman whose face impress I so much at first sight as the face of your friend , Miss Nowell . "

 " because the girl be a nobody less than nobody . there be an unpleasant kind of mystery about her birth . "

" I should like uncle Gil to love Marian just as I love she . she be the dear girl in the world . when we have a juvenile party last winter , it be Marian who dress the Christmas - tree every bit ; and she play the piano for we all the evening , do not   she , mamma ? "

 they be at home by this time , within the ground of a handsome red - brick house of the early georgian era , which have be the property of the Listers ever since it be build . without , the garden be a picture of neatness and order ; within , everything be solid and comfortable : the furniture of a somewhat ponderous and explode fashion , but handsome withal , and brighten here and there by some concession to modern notion of elegance or ease a dainty little table for book , a luxurious arm - chair , and so on .

Gilbert Fenton pretend to be profoundly interested in the thrilling question of drainage , deep or superficial , and seem to enter unreservedly into every discussion of the late invention or improvement in agricultural machinery ; and in the mean time he really like the repose of the country , and appreciate the vary charm of landscape and atmosphere with a fervour unfelt by the man who have be bear and rear amidst those pastoral scene . 

 the two man smoke their cigar together in a quietly companionable spirit , stroll about the garden and farm , drop out a sentence now and then , and anon fall into a lazy reverie , each ponder upon his own affair Gilbert meditating transaction with foreign house , risky bargain with trader of doubtful solvency , or hazardous investment in stock , as the case might be ; the gentleman farmer ruminate upon the chance of a good harvest , or the probable value of his Scotch short - horn . 

 Mr. Lister have prefer lounging about the farm with a cigar in his mouth to attendance at church upon this particular Sunday evening . he have finish his customary round of inspection by this time , and be sit by one of the open window of the drawing - room , with his body in one luxurious chair , and his leg extend upon another , deep in the study of the Gardener 's Chronicle , which he fling aside upon the appearance of his family . 

 " well , Toddlekins , " he cry to the little girl , " I hope you be very attentive to the sermon ; listen for two , and make up for your lazy dad . that be a vicarious kind of devotion that ought to be permit occasionally to a hard - work fellow like I . I be glad you have   come back to give we some tea , Belle . do not   go upstairs ; let Susan carry up your bonnet and shawl . it be nearly nine o'clock . toddlekin want her tea before she go to bed . " 

 " Lucy have have her tea in the nursery , " say Mrs. Lister , as she take her seat before the cup and saucer . 

 " but she will have some more with papa , " reply Martin , who have an amiable knack of spoil his child . there be only two this bright fair - haired Lucy , aged nine , and a sturdy boy of seven . 

 they sip their tea , and talk a little about who have be at church and who have not be , and the room be fill with that atmosphere of dulness which seem to prevail in such household upon a summer Sunday evening ; a kind of palpable emptiness which set a man speculate how many year he may have to live , and how many such sunday he may have to spend . he be apt to end by wonder a little whether life be really worth the trouble it cost , when almost the good thing that can come of it be a condition of comfortable torpor like this . 

 Gilbert Fenton put down his cup and go over to one of the open window . it be nearly as dark as it be likely to be that midsummer night . a new moon be shin faintly in the clear evening sky ; and here and there a solitary star shine with a tremulous brightness . the shadow of the tree make spot of solemn darkness on the wide lawn before the window , and a warm faint sweetness come from the crowded flower - bed , where all the flower in this light be of one grayish silvery hue . 

 " it be almost too warm an evening for the house , " say Gilbert ; " I think I will take a stroll . " 

 " I would come with you , old fellow , but I have   be all round the farm , and I be dead beat , " say good - natured Martin Lister . 

 " thank , Martin ; I would not   think of disturb you . you look the picture of comfort in that easy - chair . I shall only stay long enough to finish a cigar . " 

 he walk slowly across the lawn a noble stretch of level greensward with dark spread cedar and fine old beech scatter about it ; he walk slowly towards the gate , light his cigar as he go , and think . he be think of his past life , and of his future . what be it to be ? a dull hackneyed course of money - making , chequer only by the dreary vicissitude of trade , and brighten only by such selfish pleasure as constitute the recreation of a business man an occasional dinner at Blackwall or Richmond , a week 's shooting in the autumn , a little easy - go hunting in the winter , a hurried scamper over some of the beat continental road , or a fortnight at a german spa ? these have be his pleasure hitherto , and he have find life pleasant enough . perhaps he have be too busy to question the pleasantness of these thing . it be only now that he find himself away from the familiar arena of his daily life , with neither employment nor distraction , that he be able to look back upon his career deliberately , and ask himself whether it be one that he could go on live without weariness for the remainder of his day . 

 he have be at this time a little more than seven year in business . he have be breed - up with no expectation of ever have to take his place in the counting - house , have be educate at Eton and Oxford , and have be teach to anticipate a handsome fortune from his father . all these expectation have be disappoint by Mr. Fenton 's sudden death at a period of great commercial disturbance . the business be find in a state of entanglement that be very near insolvency ; and wise friend tell Gilbert Fenton that the only hope of come well out of these perplexity lay with himself . the business be too good to be sacrifice , and the business be all his father have leave behind he , with the exception of a houseful of handsome furniture , two or three carriage , and a couple of pair of horse , which be sell by auction within a few week of the funeral . 

 Gilbert Fenton take upon himself the management of the business . he have a clear comprehensive intellect , which adapt itself very easily to commerce . he put his shoulder to the wheel with a will , and work for the first three year of his business career as it be not give to many man to work in the course of their life . by that time the ship have be steer clear of all rock and quicksand , and ride the commercial water gallantly . Gilbert be not a rich man , but be in a fair way to become a rich man ; and the name of Fenton stand as high as in the palmy day of his father 's career .

Gilbert have only himself to work for . at first he have work for the sake of his dead father 's honour and repute ; later he fall into a groove , like other man , and work for the love of money - make not with any sordid love of money , but with that natural desire to accumulate which grow out of a business career . 

 to - night he be in an unusually thoughtful humour , and incline to weigh thing in the balance with a doubtfulness as to their value which be new to he . the complete idleness and emptiness of his life in the country have make he meditative . be it worth live , that monotonous business life of his ? would not the time soon come in which its dreariness would oppress he as the dulness of Lidford House have oppress he to - night ? his youth be fast go nay , have it not indeed go from he for ever ? have not youth leave he all at once when he begin his commercial career ? and the pleasure that have be fresh enough within the last few year be rapidly grow stale . he know the german spa , the pine - grove where the band play , the gambling - saloon and their company , by heart , though he have never stay more than a fortnight at any one of they . he have exhaust Brittany and the South of France in these rapid scamper ; skim the cream of their novelty , at any rate . he do not care very much for field - sport , and hunt and shoot in a jog - trot safe kind of way , with a view to the benefit of his health , which savour of old bachelorhood . and as for the rest of his pleasure the social rubber at his club , the Blackwall or Richmond dinner it seem only custom that make they agreeable .

I have a good mind to sell the business , now that it be worth sell , and go in for the Bar after all , late as it be . "

 CHAPTER II 

 MARIAN 'S STORY 


 the day pass , and there be no more dulness or emptiness for Gilbert Fenton in his life at Lidford . he go every day to the white - walled cottage on the green . it be easy enough to find some fresh excuse for each visit a book or a piece of music which he have recommend to Miss Nowell , and have procure from London for she , or something of an equally frivolous character . the Captain be always cordial , always pleased to see he . his visit be generally make in the evening ; and it be his delight to linger over the pretty little round table by the bow - window , drink tea dispense by Marian . the bright home - like room , the lovely face turn so trustingly to his ; these be the thing which make that fair vision of the future that haunt he so often now . he fancy himself the master of some pretty villa in the suburb at Kingston or Twickenham , perhaps with a garden slop down to the water 's edge , a lawn on which he and his wife and some choose friend might sit after dinner in the long summer evening , sip their claret or their tea , as the case might be , and watch the last rosy glow of the sunset fade and die upon the river . he fancy himself with this girl for his wife , and the delight of go back from the dull dryasdust labour of his city life to a home in which she would bid he welcome . he behave with a due amount of caution , and do not give the young lady any reason to suspect the state of the case yet awhile . Marian be perfectly devoid of coquetry , and have no idea that this gentleman 's constant presence at the cottage could have any reference to herself . he like her uncle ; what more natural than that he should like that gallant soldier , whom Marian adore as the first of mankind ? and it be out of his liking for the Captain that he come so often .

Marian seem always pleased to see Gilbert , always interested in his society ; but she do not seem more than this , and the Captain be sorely perplexed . 

 there be a dinner - party at Lidford House during the second week of Gilbert 's acquaintance with these new friend , and Captain Sedgewick and his adopt niece be invite . 

 " they be pleasant people to have at a dinner - party , " Mrs. Lister say , when she discuss the invitation with her husband and brother ; ' so I suppose they may as well come , though I do not   want to encourage your folly , Gilbert . " 

 " my folly , as you be kind enough to call it , be not dependent on your encouragement , Belle . " 

 " then it be really a serious case , I suppose , " say Martin . 

 " I really admire Miss Nowell more than I ever admire any one before , if that be what you call a serious case , Martin . " 

 " rather like it , I think , " the other answer with a laugh . 

 the dinner be a very quiet business a couple of steady - go country gentleman , with their wife and daughter , a son or two more or less dashing and sportsmanlike in style , the rector and his wife , Captain Sedgewick and Miss Nowell .  while Marian Nowell be away at this little social gathering , Captain Sedgewick and Gilbert Fenton sit under the walnut - tree smoke their cigar , with a bottle of claret on a little iron table before they . 

 " when I come back from India fourteen year ago on the sick - list , " begin the Captain , " I go down to Brighton , a place I have be fond of in my young day , to recruit . it be in the early spring , quite out of the fashionable season , and the town be very empty . my lodging be in a dull street at the extreme east , lead away from the sea , but within sight and sound of it . the solitude and quiet of the place suit I ; and I use to walk up and down the cliff in the dusk of evening enjoy the perfect loneliness of the scene . the house I live in be a comfortable one , keep by an elderly widow who be a pattern of neatness and propriety . there be no child ; for some time no other lodger ; and the place be as quiet as the grave . all this suit I very well . I want rest , and I be get it . 

 " I have be at Brighton about a month , when the drawing - room floor over my head be take by a lady , and her little girl of about five year old . I use to hear the child 's foot pattering about the room ; but she be not a noisy child by any mean ; and when I do happen to hear her voice , it have a very pleasant sound to I . the lady be an invalid , and be a good deal of trouble , my landlady take occasion to tell I , as she have no maid of her own . her name be Nowell .

 " after this we meet almost every afternoon ; and I begin to look out for these meeting , and our quiet talk upon the solitary cliff , as the pleasantest part of my day . there be a win grace about this Mrs. Nowell 's manner that I have never see in any other woman ; and I grow to be more interested in she than I care to confess to myself . it matter little now ; and I may freely own how weak I be in those day . 

 " I could see that she be very ill , and I do not need the ominous hint of the landlady , who have contrive to question Mrs. Nowell 's doctor , to inspire I with the dread that she might never recover . I will not say that I love she ; neither the fact that she be another man 's wife , nor the fact that she be soon to die , be ever absent from my mind when I think of she . I will only say that she be more to I than any woman have ever be before , or have ever be since . it be the one sentimental episode of my life , and a very brief one .

I think the trouble and anxiety cause by his delay do a good deal towards hasten the inevitable end ; but she bear her grief very quietly , and never utter a complaint of he in my hearing . she pay her way regularly enough for a considerable time , and then all at once break down , and confess to the landlady that she have not a shilling more in the world . the woman be a hard creature , and tell she that if that be the case , she must find some other lodging , and immediately . I hear this , not from Mrs. Nowell , but from the landlady , who seem to consider her conduct thoroughly justify by the high code of moral . she be a lone unprotected woman , and how be she to pay her rent and taxis if her good floor be occupy by a non - pay tenant ? 

 " I be by no mean a rich man ; but I could not endure to think of that helpless die creature thrust out into the street ; and I tell my landlady that I would be answerable for Mrs. Nowell 's rent , and for the daily expense incur on her behalf .  " Heaven grant that she may love you as you deserve to be love by she ! " Captain Sedgewick answer heartily . 

 he think it would be the good thing that could happen to his darling to become this young man 's wife , and he have a notion that a simple , inexperienced girl could scarcely help respond to the hope of such a lover . to his mind Gilbert Fenton seem eminently adapt to win a woman 's heart . he forget the fatality that belong to these thing , and that a man may have every good gift , and yet just miss the magic power to touch one woman 's heart . 

 CHAPTER III 

 ACCEPTED 


 Mr. Fenton linger another week at Lidford , with imminent peril to the safe conduct of affair at his office in Great St. Helens . he could not tear himself away just yet . he feel that he must have some more definite understanding of his position before he go back to London ; and in the meantime he ponder with a dangerous delight upon that sunny vision of a suburban villa to which Marian should welcome he when his day 's work be do . 

 he go every day to the cottage , and he bear himself in no manner like a reject lover . he be indeed very hopeful as to the issue of his wooing . he know that Marian Nowell 's heart be free , that there be no rival image to be displace before his own could reign there , and he think that it must go hard with he if he do not win her love . 

 so Marian see he every day , and have to listen to the Captain 's praise of he pretty frequently during his absence . and Captain Sedgewick 's talk about Gilbert Fenton generally close with a regretful sigh , the meaning of which have grow very clear to Marian . 

 she think about her uncle 's word and look and sigh a good deal in the quiet of her own room . what be there she would not do for the love of that dearest and noblest of man ? marry a man she dislike ? no , that be a sin from which the girl 's pure mind would have recoil instinctively . but she do like Gilbert Fenton love he perhaps though she have never confess as much to herself . 

 this calm friendship might really be love after all ; not quite such love as she have read of in novel and poem , where the passion be always render desperate by the oppose influence of adverse circumstance and unkind kindre ; but a tranquil sentiment , a dull , slow , smoulder fire , that need only some sudden wind of jealousy or misfortune to fan it into a flame . 

 she know that his society be pleasant to she , that she would miss he very much when he leave Lidford ; and when she try to fancy he reconcile to her rejection of he , and return to London to transfer his affection to some other woman , the thought be very obnoxious to she . he have not flatter she , he have be in no way slavish in his attention to she ; but he have surround she with a kind of atmosphere of love and admiration , the charm of which no girl thus belove for the first time in her life could be quite proof against . 

 thus the story end , as romance so begin generally do end . there come a summer twilight , when Gilbert Fenton find himself once more upon the dewy lawn under the walnut - tree alone with Marian Nowell . he repeat his appeal in warmer , fonder tone than before , and with a kind of imply certainty that the answer must be a favourable one . it be something like take the fortress by storm . he have his arm round her slim waist , his lip upon her brow , before she have time to consider what her answer ought to be .

"

 " Amiability , good sense , and a pure and refined nature . "

 how empty and dreary his comfortable bachelor lodging seem to he that night when he have din , and sit by the open window smoke his solitary cigar , listen to the dismal street - noise , and the monotonous roll of ceaseless wheel yonder in Oxford - street ; not care to go out to his club , care still less for opera or theatre , or any of the old way whereby he have be will not to dispose of his evening ! 

 his mind be full of Marian Nowell . all that be grave and earnest in his nature give force to this his first love . he have have flirtation in the past , of course ; but they have be no more than flirtation , and at thirty his heart be as fresh and inexperienced as a boy 's . it please he to think of Marian 's lonely position . Better , a hundred time well , that she should be thus , than fetter by tie which might come between they and perfect union . the faithful and generous protector of her childhood would of necessity always claim her love ; but beyond this one affection , she would be Gilbert 's , and Gilbert 's only . there would be no mother , no sister , to absorb her time and distract her thought from her husband , perhaps prejudice she against he . domestic life for those two must need be free from all the petty jar , the overshadow cloud no big than a man 's hand , forerunner of temp , which Mr. Fenton have hear of in many household . 

 he be never weary of think about that life which be to be . everything else he think of be now consider only in relation to that one subject . he apply himself to business with a new ardour ; never before have he be so anxious to grow rich . 

 CHAPTER IV 

 JOHN SALTRAM 


 the office of Fenton and Co. in Great St. Helens be handsome , prosperous - looking premise , consist of two large outer room , where half - a - dozen indefatigable clerk sit upon high stool before ponderous mahogany desk , and write industriously all day long ; and an inner and small apartment , where there be a faded Turkey - carpet instead of the kamptulicon that cover the floor of the outer office , a couple of capacious , red - morocco - cover arm - chair , and a desk of substantial and somewhat legal design , on which Gilbert Fenton be will not to write the more important letter of the house . in all the office there be iron safe , which give one a notion of limitless wealth store away in the shape of bond and bill , if not actual gold and bank - note ; and upon all the wall there be colour and uncoloure engraving of ship frame and glaze , and catalogue of merchandise that have be sell , or be to be sell , hang loosely one on the other . besides these , there be a great many of those flimsy paper that record the state of thing on ' Change , hang here and there on the brass rail of the desk , from little hook in the wall , and in any other available spot . and in all the premise there be an air of business and prosperity , which seem to denote that Fenton and Co. be travel at a rapid pace on the high - road to fortune . 

 Gilbert Fenton sit in the inner office at noon one day about a week after his return from Lidford . he have come to business early that morning , have initial a good many account , and write half - a - dozen letter already , and have throw himself back in his easy - chair for a few minute ' idle musing musing upon that one sweet dream of his new existence , of course . from whatever point his thought start , they always drift into that channel . 

 while he be sit like this , with his hand in his pocket and his chair tilt upon its hind leg , the half - glass door open , and a gentleman come into the office a man a little over middle height , broad - shoulder , and powerfully build , with a naturally dark complexion , which have be tan still darker by sun and wind , black eye and heavy black eyebrow , a head a little bald at the top , and a face that might have be call almost ugly but for the look of intellectual power in the broad open forehead and the perfect modelling of the flexible sensitive mouth ; a remarkable face altogether , not easily to be forget by those who have once look upon it . 

 this man be John Saltram , the one intimate and choose friend of Gilbert Fenton 's youth and manhood . they have meet first at Oxford , and have seldom lose sight of each other since the old university day . they have travel a good deal together during the one idle year that have precede Gilbert 's sudden plunge into commerce . they have be up the Nile together in the course of these wandering ; and here , remote from all civilized aid , Gilbert have fall ill of a fever a long tedious business which bring he to the very point of death , and throughout which John Saltram have nurse he with a womanly tenderness and devotion that know no abatement . if this have be want to strengthen the tie between they which it be not it would have bring they close together . as it be , that dreary time of sickness and peril be only a memory which Gilbert Fenton keep in his heart of heart , never to grow less sacred to he until the end of life . 

 Mr. Saltram be a barrister , almost a briefless one at present , for his habit be desultory , not to say idle , and he have not take very kindly to the slow drudgery of the Bar . he have some money of his own , and add to his income by write for the press in a powerful trenchant manner , with a style that be like the stroke of a sledge - hammer . in spite of this literary work , for which he get very well pay , Mr. Saltram generally contrive to be in debt ; and there be few period of his life in which he be not engage more or less in the delicate operation of raise money by bill of accommodation . Habit have give he quite an artistic touch for this kind of thing , and he do his work fondly , like some enthusiastic horticulturist who give his anxious day to the bud forth of some new orchid or the production of a hitherto unobtainable tulip . it be doubtful whether money procure from any other source be ever half so sweet to this gentleman as the cash for which he pay sixty per cent to the Jews . with these proclivity he manage to rub on from year to year somehow , get about five hundred per annum in solid value out of an income of seven , and add a little annually to the rolling mass of debt which he have begin to accumulate while he be at Balliol . 

 " why , Jack , " cry Gilbert , start up from his reverie at the entrance of his friend , and greet he with a hearty handshaking , " this be an agreeable surprise ! I be ask for you at the Pnyx last night , and Joe Hawdon tell I you be away up the Danube he think , on a canoe expedition . " 

 " it be only under some utterly impossible dispensation that Joseph Hawdon will ever be right about anything . I have be on a walk expedition in Brittany , dear boy , alone , and have find myself very bad company . I start soon after you go to your sister 's , and only come back last night . that scoundrel Levison promise I seventy - five this afternoon ; but whether I shall get it out of he be a fact only know to himself and the power with which he hold communion . and be the rustic business pleasant , Gil ? do you take kindly to the syllabub and new milk , the summer sunrise over dewy field , the pretty dairy - maid , and prize pig , and daily inspection of the home - farm ? or do you find life rather dull down at Lidford ? "

 there be a good deal of company already scatter about the lawn when John Saltram and his friend be usher into the pretty drawing - room . the cheerful sound of croquet - ball come from a level stretch of grass visible from the window , and quite a little fleet of boat be jostle one another at the landing by the swiss boat - house . 

 Mrs. Branston come in from the garden to welcome they , look very pretty in a coquettish little white - chip hat with a scarlet feather , and a pale - gray silk dress loop up over an elaborately - flounce muslin petticoat . she be a slender little woman , with a brilliant complexion , sunny wave hair , and innocent blue eye ; the sort of woman whom a man would wish to shelter from all the storm of life , but whom he might scarcely care to choose for the companion of a perilous voyage .

 CHAPTER V 

 HALCYON day

 CHAPTER VI 

 SENTENCE of EXILE

 CHAPTER VIII 

 miss 


 on the 5th of July in the following year , Gilbert Fenton land in England , after nearly ten month of exile . he have find hard work to do in the colonial city , and have do it ; surmount every difficulty by a steady resolute course of action .

Gilbert Fenton be not interested in the Liverpool trade at this particular crisis . he know that he have weather the storm which have assail his own fortune , and that the future lie clear and bright before he . 

 he do not waste an hour in London , but go straight from one station to another , and be in time to catch a train for Fairleigh , the station near to Lidford . it be five o'clock in the afternoon when he arrive at this place , and charter a fly to take he over to Lidford a lovely summer afternoon . the sight of the familiar english scenery , look so exquisite in its summer glory , fill he with a pleasure that be almost akin to pain . he have often walk this road with Marian ; and as he drive along he look eagerly at every distant figure , half hop to see his darling approach he in the summer sunlight . 

 Mr. Fenton deposit his carpet - bag at the cosy village inn , where snow - white curtain flutter gaily at every window in the warm western breeze , and innumerable geranium make a gaudy blaze of scarlet against the wooden wall . he do not stop here to make any inquiry about those he have come to see . his heart be beat tumultuously in expectation of the meeting that seem so near . he alight from the fly , dismiss the driver , and walk rapidly across a field lead by a short cut to the green on which Captain Sedgewick 's house stand . this field bring he to the side of the green opposite the Captain 's cottage . he stop for a moment as he come through the little wooden gate , and look across the grass , where a regiment of geese be march towards the still pool of willow - shadow water . 

 the shutter of the upper room be closed , and there be a board above the garden - gate . the cottage be to be let . 

 Gilbert Fenton 's heart give one great throb , and then seem to cease beat altogether . he walk across the green slowly , stun by this unlooked - for blow . yes , the house be empty . the garden , which he remember in such exquisite order , have a weedy dilapidated look that seem like the decay of some considerable time . he ring the bell several time , but there be no answer ; and he be turn away from the gate with the stunned confused feeling still upon he , unable to consider what he ought to do next , when he hear himself call by his name , and see a woman look at he across the hedge of the neighbouring garden . 

 " be you wish to make any inquiry about the last occupant of Hazel Cottage , sir ? " she ask . 

 " yes , " Gilbert answer huskily , look at she in an absent unseeing way . 

 he have see she often during his visit to the cottage , busy at work in her garden , which be much small than the Captain 's , but he have never speak to she before to - day . 

 she be a maiden lady , who eke out her slender income by let a part of her miniature abode whenever an opportunity for so doing occur . the care of this cottage occupy all her day , and form the delight and glory of her life . it be a little large than a good - sized doll 's house , and furnish with spindle - legge chair and table that have be polish to the last extremity of brightness . 

 " perhaps you would be so good as to walk into my sitting - room for a few moment , sir , " say this lady , open her garden - gate . " I shall be most happy to afford you any information about your friend . " 

 " you be very good , " say Gilbert , follow she into the prim little parlour . 

 he have recover his self - possession in some degree by this time , tell himself that this desertion of Hazel Cottage involve no more than a change of residence . 

 " my name be Dodd , " say the lady , motion Mr. Fenton to a chair , " Miss Letitia Dodd . I have the pleasure of see you very often during your visit next door . I be not on visit term with Captain Sedgewick and Miss Nowell , although we bow to each other out of door . I be only a tradesman 's daughter indeed my brother be now carry on business as a butcher in Fairleigh and of course I be quite aware of the difference in our position . I be the last person to intrude myself upon my superior . " 

 " if you will be so kind as to tell I where they have go ? " Gilbert ask , eager to stop this formal statement of Miss Dodd 's social standing . 

 " where they have go ! " she repeat . " dear , dear ! then you do not know " 

 " I do not know what ? " 

 " of Captain Sedgewick 's death . " 

 " good God ! my dear old friend ! when do he die ? " 

 " at the beginning of the year . it be very sudden a fit of apoplexy . he be seize in the night , poor dear gentleman , and it be only discover when the servant go to call he in the morning . he only live two day after the seizure ; and never speak again . " 

 " and Miss Nowell what make she leave the cottage ? she be still at Lidford , I suppose ? " 

 " o dear no , Mr. Fenton . she go away altogether about a month after the Captain 's death . " 

 " where do she go ? " 

 " I can not tell you that , I do not even know that she intend leave Hazel Cottage until the day after she leave . when I see the shutter close and the board up , you might have knock I down with a feather . Miss Nowell be so much like in Lidford , and she have more than one invitation from friend to stay with they for the sake of a change after her uncle 's death ; but she would not visit anywhere . she stay quite alone in the cottage , with only the old servant . "

 " I think not . the landlord of Hazel Cottage do not know . he be my landlord also , and I be ask he about Miss Nowell when I pay my rent the other day . he say he suppose she have go away to be marry . that have be the general impression , in fact , at Lidford . People make sure that Miss Nowell have leave to be married to you . "

 he wish Miss Dodd good - afternoon , and leave she stand at the gate of her little garden , watch he with profound interest as he walk away towards the village . there be a pleasing mystery in the affair , to the mind of Miss Dodd .

 " I have come to you full of care , Mr. Marchant , " Gilbert begin ; " and the press nature of my business must excuse the lateness of my visit . " 

 " there be no occasion for any excuse . I be very glad to see you at this time . pray help yourself to some wine , there be clean glass near you ; and take some of those strawberry , on which my wife pride herself amazingly . People who live in the country all their day be oblige to give their mind to horticulture . and now , what be this care of your , Mr. Fenton ? nothing very serious , I hope . "

" 

 " then you do not know where she go on leave this place ? " 

 " not in the faint degree . her departure be altogether unexpected by we . my wife and daughter call upon her two or three time after the Captain 's death , and be even anxious that she should come here to stay for a short time ; but she would not do that . she seem grateful , and touch by their anxiety about she , but they could not bring she to talk of her future . " 

 " and she tell they nothing of her intention to leave Lidford ? " 

 " not a word . " 

 this be all that Gilbert Fenton could learn . his interview with the Rector last some time long ; but it tell he nothing . whom next could he question ? he know all Marian 's friend , and he spend the next day in call upon they , but with the same result ; no one could tell he her reason for leave Hazel Cottage , or where she have go .

 " the poor dear young lady feel the Captain 's loss dreadfully ; as well she might , when they have be so fond of each other , " Sarah Down say , in answer to one of Gilbert 's inquiry . " I never know any one grieve so deeply . she would not   go anywhere , and she could not   bear to see any one who come to see she . she use to shut herself up in the Captain 's room day after day , kneel by his bedside , and cry as if her heart would break . I know she have be very fond of two young lady that she go to school with at Lidford , whose father live in London ; and I think it be to their house she be go . I ask she if it be , and she say yes . she make arrangement with the landlord about sell the furniture . he be an auctioneer himself , and there be no difficulty about that . the money be to be send to she at a post - office in London . I wonder at that , but she say it be well so . she pay every sixpence that be owing , and give I a handsome present over and above my wage ; though I do not   want to take anything from she , poor dear young lady , know that there be very little leave after the Captain 's death , except the furniture , which be not   likely to bring much . and so she go away about two day after she first mention that she be go to leave Lidford . it be all very sudden , and I do not   think she bid good - bye to any one in the place . she seem quite break down with grief in those two last day . I shall never forget her poor pale face when she get into the fly . " 

 " how do she go ? from the station here ? " 

 " I do not   know anything about that , except that the fly come to the cottage for she and her luggage . I want to go to the station with she , to see she off , but she would not   let I . "

 " only when I speak about you , sir . I use to try to comfort she , tell she she have you still leave to care for she , and to make up for he she would lose . but she use to look at I in a strange pitiful sort of way , and shake her head . ' I be very miserable , Sarah , ' she would say to I ; ' I be quite alone in the world now my dear uncle be go , and I do not   know what to do . ' I tell she she ought to look forward to the time when she would be marry , and would have a happy home of her own ; but I could never get she to talk of that . "

 " the name be Bruce , sir ; and they live , or they use to live at that time , in St. John's - wood . I have hear Miss Nowell say that , but I do not   know the name of the street or number of the house . "

 " yes , sir , that be true . but it seem natural enough that she should like to be alone on account of her grief . " 

 " there must have be some reason for her silence towards I , Sarah . she could not have act so cruelly without some powerful motive . Heaven only know what it may have be . the business of my life will be to find she to see her face to face once more , and hear the explanation of her conduct from her own lip . " 

 he thank the woman for her information , slip a sovereign into her hand , and depart . he call upon the proprietor of Hazel Cottage , an auctioneer , surveyor , and house - agent in the high - street of Fairleigh , but could obtain no fresh tiding from this gentleman , except the fact that the money realise by the Captain 's furniture have be send to Miss Nowell at a post - office in the City , and have be duly acknowledge by she , after a delay of about a week . the auctioneer show Gilbert the letter of receipt , which be word in a very formal business - like manner , and bear no address but " London . " the sight of the familiar hand give he a sharp pang . o God , how he have languish for a letter in that handwriting ! 

 he have nothing more to do after this in the neighbourhood of Lidford , except to pay a pious visit to the Captain 's grave , where a handsome slab of granite record the virtue of the dead . it lie in the prettiest , most retired part of the churchyard , half - hide under a wide - spread yew . Gilbert Fenton sit down upon a low wall near at hand for a long time , brood over his broken life , and wish himself at rest beneath that solemn shelter . 

 " she never love I , " he say to himself bitterly . " I shut my eye obstinately to the truth , or I might have discover the secret of her indifference by a hundred sign and token . I fancy that a man who love a woman as I love she must succeed in win her heart at last . and I accept her girlish trust in I , her innocent gratitude for my attention , as the evidence of her love . even at the last , when she want to release I , I would not understand . I do not expect to be love as I love she . I would have give so much , and be content to take so little . what be there I would not have do what sacrifice of my own pride that I would not have happily make to win she ! o my darling , even in your desertion of I you might have trust I well than this ! you would have find I fond and faithful through every trial , your friend in spite of every wrong . " 

 he kneel down by the grave , and press his lip to the granite on which George Sedgewick 's name be chisel . 

 " I owe it to the dead to discover her fate , " he say to himself , as he rise from that reverent attitude . " I owe it to the dead to penetrate the secret of her new life , to assure myself that she be happy , and have fall under no fatal influence . " 

 the Listers be still abroad , and Gilbert be very glad that it be so . it would have excruciate he to hear his sister 's comment on Marian 's conduct , and to perceive the suppress exultation with which she would most likely have discuss this unhappy termination to an engagement which have be enter on in utter disregard of her counsel . 

 CHAPTER IX 

 JOHN SALTRAM 'S advice 


 Mr. Fenton discover the Bruce family in Boundary - road , St. John's - wood , after a good deal of trouble . but they could tell he nothing of their dear friend Miss Nowell , of whom they speak with the warm regard . they have never see she since they have leave the school at Lidford , where they have be boarder , and she a daily pupil . they have not even hear of Captain Sedgewick 's death . 

 Gilbert ask these young lady if they know of any other acquaintance of Marian 's living in or near London . they both answer promptly in the negative . the school be a small one , and they have be the only pupil who come from town ; nor have they ever hear marian speak of any London friend . 

 thus end Mr. Fenton 's inquiry in this direction , leave he no wise than when he leave Lidford . he have now exhaust every possible channel by which he might obtain information . the ground lie open before he , and there be nothing leave for he but publicity . he take an advertisement to the Times office that afternoon , and pay for six insertion in the second column : 

 " Miss MARIAN NOWELL , late of Lidford , Midlandshire , be request to communicate immediately with G.F. , Post - office , Wigmore - street , to whom her silence have cause extreme anxiety . she may rely upon the advertiser 's friendship and fidelity under all possible circumstance . " 

 Gilbert feel a little more hopeful after have do this . he fancy this advertisement must need bring he some tiding of his lost love . the mystery might be happily solve after all , and Marian prove true to he . he try to persuade himself that this be possible ; but it be very difficult to reconcile her line of conduct with the fact of her regard for he . 

 in the evening he go to the Temple , eager to see John Saltram , from whom he have no intention to keep the secret of his trouble . he find his friend at home , writing , with his desk push against the open window , and the dust and shabbiness of his room dismally obvious in the hot July sunshine . he start up as Gilbert enter , and the dark face grow suddenly pale . 

 " you take I by surprise , " he say . " I do not   know you be in England . " 

 " I only land two day ago , " answer Gilbert , as they shake hand . " I daresay I startle you a little , dear old fellow , come in upon you without a moment 's notice , when you fancy I be at the Antipodes . but , you see , I hunt you up directly I be free . " 

 " you have do well out yonder , I hope , Gilbert ? " 

 " yes ; everything have go well enough with I in business . but my come home have be a dreary one . " 

 " how be that ? " 

 " Captain Sedgewick be dead , and Marian Nowell be lose . " 

 " Lost ! what do you mean by that ? " 

 Mr. Fenton tell his friend all that have befall he since his arrival in England . 

 " I come to you for counsel and help , John , " he say , when he have finish his story . 

 " I will give you my help , so far as it be possible for one man to help another in such a business , and my counsel in all honesty , " answer John Saltram ; " but I doubt if you will be incline to receive it . " 

 " why should you doubt that ? " 

 " because it be not likely to agree with your own idea . " 

 " speak out , John . " 

 " I think that if Miss Nowell have really love you , she would never have take this step . I think that she must have leave Lidford in order to escape from her engagement , perhaps expect your early return . I believe your pursuit of she can only end in failure and disappointment ; and although I be ready to assist you in any manner you wish , I warn you against sacrifice your life to a delusion . " 

 " it be not under the delusion that Marian Nowell love I that I be go to search for she , " Gilbert Fenton say slowly , after an interval of silence . " I be not so weak as to believe that after what have happen , though I have try to argue with myself , only this afternoon , that she may still be true to I and that there may have be some hide reason for her conduct . grant that she wish to escape from her engagement , she might have trust to my honour to give she a prompt release the moment I become acquainted with the real state of her feeling . there must have be some strong influence than this at work when she leave Lidford . I want to know the true cause of that hurried departure , John . I want to be sure that Marian Nowell be happy , and in safe hand . " 

 " by what mean do you hope to discover this ? " 

 " I rely a good deal upon repeat advertisement in the Times . they may bring I tiding of Marian if not directly , from some person who have see she since she leave Lidford . " 

 " if she really wish to hide herself from you , she would most likely change her name . " 

 " why should she wish to hide herself from I ? she must know that she might trust I . of her own free will she would never do this cruel thing . there must have be some secret influence at work upon my darling 's mind . it shall be my business to discover what that influence be ; or , in plainer word still , to discover the man who have rob I of Marian Nowell 's heart . " 

 " it come to that , then , " say John Saltram . " you suspect some unknown rival ? " 

 " yes ; that be the most natural conclusion to arrive at . and yet heaven know how unwillingly I take that into consideration . " 

 " there be no particular person whom you suspect ? " 

 " no one . " 

 " if there should be no result from your advertisement , what will you do ? " 

 " I can not tell you just yet . unless I get some kind of clue , the business will seem a hopeless one . but I can not imagine that the advertisement will fail completely . if she leave Lidford to be marry , there must be some record of her marriage . should my first advertisement fail , my next shall be insert with a view to discover such a record . " 

 " and if , after infinite trouble , you should find she the wife of another man , what reward would you have for your waste time and lose labour ? " 

 " the happiness of know she to be in a safe and honourable position . I love she too dearly to remain in ignorance of her fate . " 

 " well , Gilbert , I know that good advice be generally throw away in such a case as this ; but I have a fix opinion on the subject . to my mind , there be only one wise course open to you , and that be , to let this thing alone , and resign yourself to the inevitable . I acknowledge that Miss Nowell be eminently worthy of your affection ; but you know the old song ' if she be not fair to I , what care I how fair she be . ' there be plenty of woman in the world . the choice be wide enough . " 

 " not for I , John . Marian Nowell be the only woman I have ever love , the only woman I ever can love . " 

 " my dear boy , it be so natural for you to believe that just now ; and a year hence you will think so differently ! "

I know that my life be break that this blow have leave I nothing to hope for or to live for , except the hope of find the girl who have wrong I . I will not   weary you with lamentation . my talk have be entirely of self since I come into this room . tell I your own affair , Jack , old friend . how have the world go with you since we part at Liverpool last year ? " 

 " not too smoothly . my financial position become a little more obscure and difficult of comprehension every year , as you know ; but I rub on somehow . I have be work at literature like a galley - slave ; have contribute no end of stuff to the Quarterlies ; and be engage upon a book , yes Gil , positively a book , which I hope may do great thing for I if ever I can finish it . " 

 " be it a novel ? " 

 " a novel ! no ! " cry John Saltram , with a wry face ; " it be the romance of reality I deal with . my book be a Life of Jonathan Swift . he be always a favourite study of mine , you know , that brilliant , unprincipled , intolerant , cynical , irresistible , miserable man . Scott 's biography seem to I to give but a tame picture , and other be only sketch . mine will be a pre - raphaelite study faithful as a photograph , careful as a miniature on ivory , and life - size . "

 " a little more so , if possible . poor old Michael Branston be dead go off the hook rather suddenly about a month ago . the widow look amazingly pretty in her weed . " 

 " and you will marry she , I suppose , Jack , as soon as her mourning be over ? " 

 " well , yes ; it be on the card , " John Saltram say , in an indifferent tone . 

 " why , how you say that ! be there any doubt as to the lady 's fortune ? " 

 " o no ; that be all square enough . Michael Branston 's will be in the Illustrated London News ; the personalty swear under a hundred and twenty thousand , all leave to the widow , besides real property a house in Cavendish Square , the villa at Maidenhead , and a place near Leamington . " 

 " it would be a splendid match for you , Jack . " 

 " Splendid , of course . an unprecedented stroke of luck for such a fellow as i. yet I doubt very much if I be quite the man for that sort of life . I should be apt to fancy it a kind of gild slavery , I think , Gil , and there would be some danger of my kicking off the chain . " 

 " but you like Mrs. Branston , do not   you , Jack ? " 

 " like she ? yes , I like she too well to deceive she . and she would expect devoted affection from a second husband . she be full of romantic idea , school - girl theory of life which she be oblige to nip in the bud when she go to the altar with old Branston , but which have burst into flower now that she be free . " 

 " have you see she often since her husband 's death ? " 

 " only twice ; once immediately after the funeral , and again yesterday . she be live in Cavendish Square just now . " 

 " I hope you will marry she . I should like to see you safe in smooth water , and with some purpose in life . I should like to see you turn your back upon the loneliness of these dreary chamber . "
