 " Whereabouts ? " 

 " sometimes in one direction , sometimes in another ; wherever my truant fancy lead I . I prefer such spot as be most remote from the haunt of man , unknown to cockney ; and so long as there be a river within reach of my lodging , I can make myself tolerably happy with a punt and a fishing - rod , and contrive to forget my care . " 

 " you have not be to Lidford since I leave England , I suppose ? " 

 " yes ; I be at Heatherly a week or two in the winter . poor old David Forster would not let I alone until I go down to he . he be ill , and in a very dismal condition altogether , abandon by the rest of his crony , and a close prisoner in the house which have so many painful association for he . it be a work of charity to bear he company . " 

 " do you see Captain Sedgewick , or Marian , while you be down there ? " 

 " no . I should have like to have call upon the kind old Captain ; but Forster be unconscionably exact , there be no get away from he . " 

 Gilbert stop with his friend until late that night , smoking and drink a mild mixture of brandy and soda - water , and talk of the thing that have be do on this side of the globe while he have be on the other . no more be say about Marian , or Gilbert 's plan for the future . in his own mind that one subject reign supreme , shut out every other thought ; but he do not want to make himself a nuisance to John Saltram , and he know that there be bound to the endurance of which friendship be capable . 

 the two friend seem cheerful enough as they smoke their cigar in the summer dusk , the quiet of the flagged court below rarely break by a pass footfall . it be the pleasantest evening which Gilbert Fenton have spend for a long time , in spite of the heavy burden on his mind , in spite of the depressing view which Mr. Saltram take of his position . 

 " dear old John , " he say , as they shake hand at parting , " I can not tell you what a happiness it have be to I to see you again . we be never separate so long before since the day when I eat my first dinner at Balliol . " 

 the other seem touch by this expression of regard , but disinclined to betray his emotion , after the manner of Englishmen on such occasion . 

 " my dear Gilbert , it ought to be very pleasant to I to hear that . but I doubt if I be worthy of so much . as far as my own liking for you go , there be no inequality between we ; but you be a well fellow than I be by a long way , and be not likely to profit much in the long - run by your friendship for a reprobate like I . " 

 " that be all nonsense , John . that kind of vague self - accusation mean nothing . I have no doubt I shall live to see you a great man , and to be proud enough of be able to claim you as the choose friend of my youth . Mr. Branston 's death have clear the way for you . the chance of a distinguished future be within your grasp . " 

 " the chance within my grasp ! yes . my dear Gilbert , I tell you there be some man for whom everything in this world come too late . " 

 " what do you mean by that ? " 

 " only that I doubt if you will ever see I Adela Branston 's husband . " 

 " I can not   understand you , John . " 

 " my dear fellow , there be nothing strange in that . there be time when I can not understand myself . " 

 CHAPTER x 

 JACOB NOWELL 


 the day go by , and bring Gilbert Fenton no reply to his advertisement . he call at the post - office morning and evening , only to find the same result ; and a dull blank feeling , a kind of deadness of heart and mind , begin to steal over he with the progress of the day . he go through the routine of his business - life steadily enough , work as hard as he have ever work ; but it be only by a supreme effort that he could bring his mind to bear upon the detail of business all interest in his office - work be go . 

 the advertisement have appear for the sixth time , and Gilbert have frame a second , offer a reward of twenty pound for any direct evidence of the marriage of Marian Nowell ; when a letter be hand to he one evening at the post - office a letter in a common blue envelope , direct in a curious crab hand , and bear the London post - mark . 

 his heart beat loud and fast as he tear open this envelope . it contain only a half - sheet of paper , with these word write upon it in the cramped half - illegible hand which figure on the outside : 

 " the person advertise for Marian Nowell be request to call at no . 5 , Queen Anne 's Court , Wardour Street , any evening after seven . " 

 this be all . little as this brief note imply , however , Gilbert make sure that the writer must be in a position to give he some kind of information about the object of his search . it be six o'clock when he receive the communication . he go from the post - office to his lodging with his mind in a tumult of excitement , make a mere pretence of take a hasty dinner , and set off immediately afterwards for Wardour Street . 

 there be more than time for he to walk , and he hop that the walk might have some effect in reduce the fever of his mind . he do not want to present himself before stranger who , no doubt , only want to make a barter of any knowledge they possess as to Marian 's whereabouts in a state of mental excitement . the address to which he be go mystify he beyond measure . what could people live in such a place as this know of she whom he seek ? 

 he be in Wardour Street at a quarter before seven , but he have considerable trouble in find Queen Anne 's Court , and the clock of the neighbourhood be strike the hour as he turn into a narrow alley with dingy - look shop on one side and a high dead wall on the other . the gas be glimmer faintly in the window of No . 5 , and a good deal of old silver , tarnish and blacken , huddle together behind the wire - guard glass , be dimly visible in the uncertain light . there be some old jewellery too , and a little wooden bowl of sovereign or gold coin of some kind or other . 

 on a brass plate upon the door of this establishment there appear the name of Jacob Nowell , silversmith and money - changer . 

 Gilbert Fenton star in amazement at this inscription . it must need be some relative of Marian 's he be about to see . 

 he open the door , bewilder a little by this discovery , and a shrill bell give notice of his entrance to those within . a tall lanky young man , with a sallow face and sleek black hair , emerge quickly from some door in the obscure background , and ask in a sharp voice what the visitor please to want . 

 " I wish to see Mr. Nowell , the writer of a letter address to the post - office in Wigmore Street . " 

 the sallow - faced young man disappear without a word , leave Gilbert stand in the dimly light shop , where he see more old silver crowd upon shelf behind glass door , carve ebony cabinet loom out of the dusk , and here and there an old picture in a tarnish frame . on the counter there be a glass case contain foreign bank - note and gold , some curious old watch , and other trinket , a baby 's coral , a battered silver cup , and a gold snuff - box . 

 while Gilbert wait thus he hear voice in a room at the back the shrill tone of the sallow young man and a feeble old voice raise querulously and then , after a delay which seem long to his impatience , the young man reappear and tell he Mr. Nowell be ready to see he . 

 Gilbert go into the room at the end of the shop a small dark parlour , more crowded with a heterogeneous collection of plate , picture , and bric - a - brac of all kind than the shop itself . Sultry as the July evening be , there be a fire burn in the pinch rusty grate , and over this fire the owner of the room bend affectionately , with his slippere foot on the fender , and his bony hand clasp his bony knee . 

 he be an old man , with long yellowish - white hair stream from beneath a velvet skull - cap , and bright black eye deep set in a pale thin face . his nose be a sharp aquiline , and give something of a bird - like aspect to a countenance that must once have be very handsome . he be wrap in a long dressing - gown of some thick grey woollen stuff . 

 the sallow - faced young man linger by the half - glass door between the parlour and the shop , as if he would fain have remain a witness to the interview about to take place between his master and the strange ; but the old man look round at he sharply , and say , 

 " that will do , Tulliver ; you can go back to the shop . if Abrahams bring that little lot again to - night , tell he I will give five - and - nine an ounce , not a fraction more . " 

 Mr. Tulliver retire , leave the door ajar ever so little ; but the penetrate black eye of the master be quick to perceive this manoeuvre . 

 " will you be so good as to shut that door , sir , quite securely ? " he say to Gilbert . " that young man be very inquisitive ; I be afraid I have   keep he too long . People talk of old servant ; but half the robbery in the world be commit by old servant . be seat , if you please , sir . you find this room rather close , perhaps . some people do ; but I be old and chilly , and I can not   live without a fire . " 

 " I have come to you in great anxiety of mind , " say Gilbert , as he seat himself upon the only disengage chair in the room , " and with some hope that you may be able to set my mind at ease by afford I information about Miss Marian Nowell . " 

 " I can give you no information about she . " 

 " indeed ! " cry Gilbert , with a bitter pang of disappointment ; " and yet you answer my advertisement . " 

 " I do , because I have some reason to suppose this Marian Nowell may be my granddaughter . " 

 " that be quite possible . " 

 " can you tell I her father 's name ? " 

 " Percival Nowell . her mother be a Miss Lucy Geoffry . " 

 " right , " say the old man . " Percival Nowell be my only son my only child of late year . there be a girl , but she die early . he be my only son , and his mother and I be foolish enough to be proud of his good look and his clever way ; and we bring he up a gentleman , send he to an expensive school , and after that to the University , and pinch ourselves in every way for his sake . my father be a gentleman ; and it be only after I have fail as a professional man , through circumstance which I need not explain to you now , that I take to this business . I would have make any sacrifice in reason for that boy of mine . I want he to be a gentleman , and to make his way in one of the learn profession . after a great deal of chop and change , he fix upon the Bar , take chamber in the Temple , make I pay all the fee , and pretend to study . but I soon find that he be lead a wild dissipate life , and be never likely to be good for anything . he get into debt , draw bill upon I , and behave altogether in a most shameful manner . when I send for he , and remonstrate with he upon his disgraceful conduct , he tell I that I be a miser , that I spend my life in a dog - kennel for the sake of hoard money , and that I deserve nothing well than his treatment of I . I may have be well off at this time than I have care to let he know , for I have soon find out what a reckless scoundrel I have to deal with ; but if he have behave decently , he would have find I generous and indulgent enough . as it be , I tell he to go about his business , and never to expect another sixpence from I as long as he live . how he manage to exist after this , I hardly know . he be very much mix up with a disreputable lot of turf - man , and I believe he make money by bet . his mother rob I for he , I find out afterwards , and contrive to send he a good deal of money at odd time . my business as a dealer in second - hand silver be well then than it be now , and I have have so much money pass through my hand that it be pretty easy for my wife to cheat I . poor soul ! she have be dead and go these fifteen year , and I have freely forgive she . she love that young man to distraction . if he have want a step to reach the object of his wish , she would have lay herself down in the dust and let he walk over her body . I suppose it be in the nature of mother to love their son like that . well , sir , I never see my gentleman after that day . I have plenty of letter from he , all ask for money ; threaten letter , pitiful letter , letter in which he swear he would destroy himself if he do not   receive a remittance by return of post ; but I never send he a shilling . about a year after our last meeting , I receive the announcement of his marriage with Miss Geoffry . he write to tell I that , if I would allow he a decent income , he would reform and lead a steady life . that letter I do answer : to the effect that , if he choose to come here and act as my shopman , I would give he board and lodge for himself and his wife , and such wage as he should deserve . I tell he that I have give he his chance as a gentleman , and he have throw it away . I would give he the opportunity now of succeed in a humbler career by sheer industry and perseverance as I have succeed myself . if he think that I have make a fortune , there be so much the more reason for he to try his luck . this be the last letter I ever write to he . it be unanswered ; but about a year and a half afterwards there come a few line to his mother , tell she of the birth of a daughter , which be to be call Marian , after she . this last letter come from Brussels . " 

 " and do you hear no more of your son after this ? " Gilbert ask . 

 " nothing . I think his mother use to get letter from he in secret for some time ; that these fail suddenly at last ; and that anxiety about her worthless son anxiety which she try to hide from I shorten her life . she never complain , poor soul ! never mention Percy 's name until the last , when she beg I to be kind to he if he should ever come to throw himself upon my kindness . I give she my promise that , if that come to pass , he should find I a well friend to he than he deserve . it be hard to refuse the last prayer of a faithful wife who have do her duty patiently for nearly thirty year . " 

 " have you any reason to suppose your son still live ? " 

 " I have no evidence of his death . often and often , after my poor wife be go , I have sit alone here of a night thinking of he ; think that he might come in upon I at any moment ; almost listen for his footstep in the quiet of the place . but he never come . he would have find I very soft - hearted at such time . my mind change to he a good deal after his mother 's death . I use to think of he as he be in his boyhood , when Marian and I have such great hope of he , and would sit and talk of he for hour together by this fireside . an old man leave quite alone as I be have plenty of time for such thought . night after night I have fancy I hear his step , and have look up at that door expect to see he open it and come in ; but he never come . he may be dead . I suppose he be dead ; or he would have come to make another attempt at get money out of I . " 

 " you have never take any measure for find he ? " inquire Gilbert . 

 " no . if he want I , he know where I be to be find . I be a fixture . it be his business to come to I . when I see the name of Marian Nowell in your advertisement a week ago , I feel curious to know whether it could be my grandchild you be look for . I hold off till this morning , think it be not   worth my while to make any inquiry about the matter ; but I could not   get it out of my head somehow ; and it end by my answer your advertisement . I be an old man , you see , without a creature belong to I ; and it might be a comfort to I to meet with some one of my own flesh and blood . the bit of money I may leave behind I when I die will not   be much ; but it might as well go to my son 's child as to a stranger . " 

 " if your son 's child can be find , you will discover she to be well worthy of your love . yes , though she have do I a cruel wrong , I believe she to be all that be good and pure and true . " 

 " what be the wrong that she have do you ? " 

 Gilbert tell Jacob Nowell the story of his engagement , and the bitter disappointment which have befall he on his return from Australia . the old man listen with every appearance of interest . he approve of Gilbert 's notion of advertising for the particular of a possible marriage , and offer to bear his part in the expense of the search for his granddaughter . 

 Gilbert smile at this offer . 

 " you do not know what a worthless thing money be to I now , " he say , " or now lightly I hold my own trouble or loss in this matter . " 

 he leave Queen Anne 's Court soon after this , after have promise Jacob Nowell to return and report progress so soon as there should be anything worth tell . he go back to Wigmore Street heavy - hearted , depressed by the reaction that follow the vain hope which the silversmith 's letter have inspire . it matter little to he to know the antecedent of Marian 's father , while Marian 's destiny remain still hide from he . 

 CHAPTER XI 

 the marriage at wygrove 


 on the following day Gilbert Fenton take his second advertisement to the office in Printing House Square ; an advertisement offer a reward of twenty pound for any reliable information as to the marriage of Marian Nowell . a week go by , during which the advertisement appear on alternate day ; and at the end of that time there come a letter from the parish - clerk of Wygrove , a small town about forty mile far from London than Lidford , state that , on the 14th of March , John Holbrook and Marian Nowell have be marry at the church in that place . Gilbert Fenton leave London by an early train upon the morning after his receipt of this letter ; and at about three o'clock in the afternoon find himself on the outskirt of Wygrove , rather a difficult place to reach , involve a good deal of delay at out - of - the - way junction , and a six - mile journey by stage - coach from the near station . 

 it be about the dull dreariest little town to which his destiny have ever bring Gilbert Fenton , consist of a melancholy high - street , with a blank market - place , and a town hall that look as if it have not be open within the memory of man ; a grand old gothic church , much too large for the requirement of the place ; a grim square brick box inscribe " Ebenezer ; " and a few prim villa straggle off into the country . 

 on one side of the church there be a curious little old - fashioned court , wonderfully neat and clean , with house the parlour whereof be sink below the level of the pavement , after the manner of these old place . there be a great show of geranium in the casement , and a general aspect of brightness and order distinguish all these modest dwelling . it be to this court that Mr. Fenton have be direct on inquire for Thomas Stoneham , the parish - clerk , at the inn where the coach deposit he . he be fortunate enough to find Mr. Stoneham sun himself on the threshold of his domicile , smoke an after - dinner pipe . a pleasant clattering of tea - thing sound from the neat little parlour within , show that , early as it be , there be already preparation for the cup which cheer without inebriate in the Stoneham household . 

 Thomas Stoneham , support by a freshly - paint door of a vivid green and an extensive brass plate engrave with his name and function , be a personage of some dignity . he be a middle - aged man , ponderous and slow of motion , with a latent pomposity , which he render as agreeable as possible by the urbanity of his manner . he be a man of a lofty spirit , who believe in his office as something exalt above all other dignity of this earth less lucrative , of course , than a bishopric or the woolsack , and of a narrow range , but quite as important on a small scale . " the world might get on pretty well without bishop , " think Mr. Stoneham , when he ponder upon these thing as he smoke his churchwarden pipe ; " but what would become of a parish in which there be no clerk ? " 

 this gentleman , see Gilbert Fenton approach , be quick to surmise that the stranger come in answer to the letter he have write the day before . the advent of a stranger in Wygrove be so rare an occurrence , that it be natural enough for he to jump at this conclusion . 

 " I believe you be Mr. Stoneham , " say Gilbert , " and the writer of a letter in answer to an advertisement in the Times . " 

 " my name be Stoneham , sir ; I be the clerk of this parish , and have be for twenty year and more , as I think I may have state in the letter to which you refer . will you be so kind as to step inside ? " 

 Mr. Stoneham wave his hand towards the parlour , to which apartment Gilbert descend . here he find Mrs. Stoneham , a meek little sandy - haired woman , who seem to be bear down by the weight of her lord 's dignity ; and Miss Stoneham , also meek and sandy , with a great many stiff little corkscrew ringlet bud out all over her head and a sharp little inquire nose . 

 these lady would have retire on Gilbert 's entrance , but he beg they to remain ; and after a good deal of polite hesitation they consent to do so , Mrs. Stoneham resume her seat before the tea - tray , and Miss Stoneham retire to a little table by the window , where she be engage in trim a bonnet . 

 " I want to know all about this marriage , Mr. Stoneham , " Gilbert begin , when he have seat himself in a shin mahogany arm - chair by the empty fire - place . " first and foremost , I want you to tell I where Mr. and Mrs. Holbrook be now live . " 

 the parish - clerk shake his head with a stately slowness . 

 " not to be do , sir , " he say : " when Mr. and Mrs. Holbrook leave here they go the Lord know where . they go away the very day they be marry . there be a fly wait for they at the church - door , with their luggage upon it , when the ceremony be over , ready to drive they to Grangewick station . I see they get into it and drive away ; and that be every mortal thing that I know as to what become of they after they be marry in yonder church . " 

 " you do not   know who this Mr. Holbrook be ? " 

 " no more than the babe unborn , sir . he be a stranger in this place , be only here long enough to get the license for his marriage . I should take he to be a gentleman ; but he be not   a pleasant person to speak to rather stand - off - ish in his manner . he be not   the sort of man I should have choose if I have be a pretty young woman like Miss Nowell ; but there be no accounting for taste , and she seem uncommonly fond of he . I never see any one more agitated than she be when they be marry . she be cry in a quiet way all through the service , and when it be over she faint dead - off . I daresay it do seem hard to she to be marry like that , without so much as a friend to give she away . she be in mourning , too , deep mourning . " 

 " can you give I any description of this man this Mr. Holbrook ? " 

 " well , no , sir : he be an ordinary kind of person to look at ; might be any age between thirty and forty ; not a gentleman that I should have take a fancy to myself , as I say before ; but young woman be that wayward and uncertain like , there be no knowing where to have they . " 

 " be Miss Nowell long at Wygrove before her marriage ? " 

 " about three week . she lodge with Miss Long , up the town , a friend of my daughter 's . if you would like to ask any question of Miss Long , our Jemima might step round there with you presently . " 

 " I should be very glad to do so , " Gilbert answer quickly . he ask several more question ; but Mr. Stoneham could give he no information , except as to the bare fact of the marriage . Gilbert know now that the girl he have so fondly love and so entirely trust be utterly lose to he ; that he have be jilt cruelly and heartlessly , as he could but own to himself . yes , she have jilt he have in all probability never love he . he blame himself for have urge his suit too ardently , with little reference to Marian 's own feeling , with a root obstinate conviction that he need only to win she in order to insure the happiness of both . 

 have fully prove Mr. Stoneham 's inability to afford he any further help in this business , Gilbert avail himself of the fair Jemima 's willingness to ' step round " to Miss Long 's domicile with he , in the hope of obtain full information from that lady . while Miss Stoneham be engage in put on her bonnet for this expedition , the clerk propose to take Gilbert across to the church and show he the entry of the marriage in the register . " with a view to the satisfactory settlement of the reward , " Mr. Stoneham add in a fat voice , and with the air of a man to whom twenty pound more or less be an affair of very little moment . 

 Gilbert assent to this , and accompany Mr. Stoneham to a little side - door which admit they into the old church , where the light shine dimly through paint window , in which there seem more leaden framework than glass . the atmosphere of the place be cold even on this sultry July afternoon , and the vestry to which Mr. Stoneham conduct his companion have a damp mouldy smell . 

 he open a cupboard , with a good deal of jingling of a great bunch of key , and produce the register ; a grim - look volume bind in dingy leather , and calculate to inspire gloomy feeling in the mind of the bridegroom and bride who have occasion to inscribe their name therein ; a volume upon which the love and the grace who hover around the entrance to the matrimonial state have shed no ray of glamour . 

 Thomas Stoneham lay this book before Gilbert , open at the page on which Marian 's marriage be record . yes , there be the familiar signature in the fair flow hand he have love so well . it be his Marian , and no other , whom John Holbrook have marry in that gloomy old church . 

 the signature of the bridegroom be in a stiff straight hand , all the letter form with unusual precision , as if the name have be write in a slow laboured way . 

 who could this John Holbrook be ? Gilbert be quite certain that he have never hear the name at Lidford , nor could he believe that if any attachment between this man and Marian Nowell have exist before his own acquaintance with she , Captain Sedgewick would have be so dishonourable as to keep the fact a secret from he . this John Holbrook must need , therefore , be some one who have come to Lidford during Gilbert 's absence from England ; yet Sarah Down have be able to tell he of no new visitor at Hazel Cottage . 

 he copy the record of the marriage on a leaf in his pocket - book , pay Mr. Stoneham a couple of ten - pound note , and leave the church . the clerk 's daughter be wait for he in the little court outside , and they go at once to the house where Miss Nowell have lodge during her residence at Wygrove . 

 it be a house in a neat little terrace on the outskirt of the town ; a house approach by a flight of steep stone step of spotless purity , and a half - glass door , which open at once into a bright airy - look parlour , faintly perfume with rose - leaf and lavender mouldering in the china vase on the mantelpiece . here Gilbert be introduce to Miss Long , a maiden lady of uncertain age , who wear stiff band of suspiciously black hair under an imposing structure of lace and artificial flower , and a rusty black - silk dress , the body of which fit so tightly as to seem like a kind of armour . this lady receive Mr. Fenton very graciously , and declare herself quite ready to give he any information in her power about Miss Nowell . 

 it happen unfortunately , however , that her power be of a most limited extent . 

 " a sweet young lady never live than Miss Nowell , " she say . " I have   have a great many people occupy these apartment since my father 's death leave I throw upon my own resource . I have   have lodger that I might call permanent , in a manner of speak ; but I never have any one that I take to as I take to Miss Nowell , though she be hardly with I three week from first to last . " 

 " do she seem happy in her mind during that time ? " Gilbert ask . 

 " well , no ; I can not say that she do . I should have expect to see a young lady that be go to be married to the man she love much more cheerful and hopeful about the future than Miss Nowell be . she tell I that her uncle have not be dead many week , and I think at first that this be the only grief she have on her mind ; but after some time , when I find she very low and downhearted , and have win upon she to trust I almost as if I have be an old friend , she own to I that she have behave very badly to a gentleman she have be engage to , and that the thought of her wickedness to he prey upon her mind . ' I do not   think any good can ever come of my marriage , Miss Long , ' she say to I ; ' I think I must surely be punish for my falsehood to the good man who love I so truly . but there be some thing in life that seem like fate . they come upon we in a moment , and we have no strength to fight against they . I believe it be my fate to love John Holbrook . there be nothing in this world I could refuse to do for his sake . if he have ask I for my life , I must have give it to he as freely as I give he my love . from the first hour in which I see he he be my master . ' " 

 " this Mr. Holbrook be very fond of she , I suppose ? " 

 " I daresay he be , sir ; but he be not a man that show his feeling very much . they use to go for long walk together , though it be March and cold windy weather , and she always seem happy when he bring her home . he come every evening to drink tea with she , and I use to hear they talk as I sit at work in the next room . she be happy enough when he be with she . it be only when she be alone that she would give way to low spirit and gloomy thought about the future . " 

 " do she ever tell you anything about Mr. Holbrook his position or profession ? how long she have know he ? how and where they have first meet ? " 

 " no , sir . she tell I once that he be not rich ; I think that be about all she ever say of he , except when she speak of his influence over she , and her trust in he . " 

 " have you any idea where they be go to live after their marriage ? " 

 " I can not tell you the name of the place . Miss Nowell say that a friend of Mr. Holbrook 's be go to lend he an old farm - house in a very pretty part of the country . it would be very lonely , she say , and her husband would have sometimes to leave she to attend to his business in London ; but she would not mind that . ' some day , I daresay , he will let I live in London with he , ' she say ; ' but I do not   like to ask he that yet . ' " 

 " do she drop no hint as to the whereabouts of this place to which they be go ? " 

 " it be somewhere in Hampshire ; that be all I can remember . " 

 " I would give a great deal to know more , " Gilbert say with a sigh . " in what manner do this Mr. Holbrook impress you ? you be interested in the young lady , and would therefore naturally be interested in her lover . do he strike you as worthy of she ? " 

 " I can not say that he do , sir , " Miss Long answer doubtfully . " I could see that he have great power over she , though his manner to she be always very gentle ; but I can not say that I take to he myself . I daresay he be a very clever man ; but he have a cold proud way that keep one at a distance from he , and I seem to know no more of he at the last than I have know on the first day I see he . I believe he love Miss Nowell , and that be about all the good I do believe of he . " 

 after this , there be no more to be ask of Miss Long ; so Gilbert thank she for her civility , and bade good evening at once to she and to Miss Stoneham . there be time for he to catch the last coach to Grangewick station . he determine upon go from Grangewick to Lidford , instead of return to London . he want , if possible , to find out something more about this man Holbrook , who must surely have be know to some one at Lidford during his secret courtship of Marian Nowell . 

 he waste two day at Lidford , make inquiry on this subject , in as quiet a manner as possible and in every imaginable quarter ; but without the slight result . no one either at Lidford or Fairleigh have ever hear of Mr. Holbrook . 

 Gilbert 's last inquiry be make in a singular direction . after exhaust every likely channel of information , he have a few hour leave before the departure of the fast train by which he have determine to return to London ; and this leisure he devote to a visit to Heatherly Park , in the chance of find Sir David Forster at home . it be just possible that Mr. Holbrook might be one of Sir David 's innumerable bachelor acquaintance . 

 Gilbert walk from Lidford to Heatherly by that romantic woodland path by which he have go with Marian and her uncle on the bright September afternoon when he first see Sir David 's house . the solitary walk awaken very bitter thought ; the memory of those hope which have then make the sunshine of his life , and without which existence seem a weary purposeless journey across a desert land . 

 Sir David be at home , the woman at the lodge tell he ; and he go on to the house , and ring a great clanging bell , which make an alarming clamour in the utter stillness of the place . 

 a gray - haired old servant answer the summon , and usher Gilbert into the state drawing - room , an apartment with a lofty arched roof , eight long window , and a generally ecclesiastical aspect , which be more suggestive of solemn grandeur than of domestic comfort . 

 here Gilbert wait for about ten minute , at the end of which time the man return , to request that he would be so kind as to go to Sir David 's study . his master be something of an invalid , the man tell Gilbert . 

 they go through the billiard - room to a very snug little apartment , with dark - panel wall and one large window opening upon a rose - garden on the southern side of the house . there be a ponderous carve - oak bookcase on one side of the room ; on all the other the paraphernalia of sporting gunnery and fishing - tackle , small - sword , whip , and boxing - glove artistically arrange against the panelling ; and over the mantelpiece an elaborate collection of meerschaum pipe . through a half - open door Gilbert catch a glimpse of a comfortable bedchamber lead out of this room . 

 Sir David be sit on a low easy - chair near the window , with one leg support on a luxuriously - cushion rest , invent for the relief of gouty subject . although not yet forty , the baronet be a chronic sufferer from this complaint . 

 " my dear Mr. Fenton , how good of you to come to I ! " he exclaim , shake hand very cordially with Gilbert . " here I be , lay by the heel in this dreary old place , and quite alone . you can not   imagine what a treat it be to see a friendly intelligent face from the outer world . " 

 " the purpose of my visit be such a purely selfish one , that I be really ashamed to receive such a kindly greeting , Sir David . if I have know you be here and an invalid , I should have gladly come to see you ; but I do not   know it . I have be at Lidford on a matter of business for the last two day ; and I come here on the hazard of find you , and with a faint hope that you might be able to give I some help in an affair which be supremely important to I . " 

 Sir David Forster look at Gilbert Fenton curiously for a moment , and then take up an empty meerschaum that lie upon a little table near he , and begin to fill it with a thoughtful air . Gilbert have drop into an arm - chair on the opposite side of the open window , and be watch the baronet 's face , puzzle a little by that curious transient expression which have just flit across it . 

 " what be the business ? " Sir David ask presently ; " and how can I be of use to you ? " 

 " I think you know all about my engagement to Miss Nowell , when I be here last September , Sir David , " Gilbert begin presently . 

 " yes , Saltram tell I you be engage ; not but what it be easy enough to see how the land lie , without any telling . " 

 " Miss Nowell have jilt I . I love she too dearly to be able to entertain any vindictive feeling against she ; but I do feel vindictively dispose towards the man who have rob I of she , for I know that only a very powerful influence would have induce she to break faith with I ; and this man must need have know the dishonourable thing he be do when he tempt she away from I . I want to know who he be , Sir David , and how he come to acquire such an influence over my plighted wife . " 

 " my dear Fenton , you be go on so fast ! you say Miss Nowell have jilt you . she be married to some one else , then , I suppose ? " 

 " she be married to a Mr. Holbrook . I come to Lidford the night before last , with the hope of find out something about he ; but all my endeavour have result in failure . it strike I at last , as a kind of forlorn hope , that this Mr. Holbrook might possibly be one of your autumnal visitor ; and I come here to ask you that question . " 

 " no , " answer the baronet ; " I have have no visitor call Holbrook . be the name quite strange to yourself ? " 

 " entirely strange . " 

 " and this Mr. Holbrook be now Miss Nowell 's husband ? and you want to know who he be ? with what end ? " 

 " I want to find the man who have do I the deadly wrong one man can do another . " 

 " my dear fellow , do not   you see that it be fate , and not Mr. Holbrook , that have do you this wrong ? if Miss Nowell have really love you as she ought to have love you , it would have be quite impossible for she to be tempt away from you . it be her destiny to marry this Holbrook , rely upon it ; and have you be on the spot to protect your own interest , the result would have be just the same . believe I , I be very sorry for you , and can fully sympathise with your feeling in this business ; but I can not see what good could possibly arise out of a meeting between you and your fortunate rival . the day of duelling be past ; and even if it be not so , I think you be too generous to seek to deprive Miss Nowell of her husband . "

 " my dear fellow , you know the old saying . a man who be in love think everything fair . there be no such thing as honour in such a case as this . of course , I do not   want to defend this Holbrook ; I only want to awaken your sens to the absurdity of any vindictive pursuit of the man . if the lady do not love you , believe I you be well out of the business .  Gilbert smile , a melancholy incredulous smile . Sir David 's advice be only the echo of John Saltram 's counsel the counsel which he would receive from every man of the world , no doubt the counsel which he himself would most likely have give to a friend under the same circumstance .

" whatever I have to leave will be her . it may be little or much I will not   speak about that ; but I have   live a hard life , and save where other man would have spend .  " would it not be a good plan to put an advertisement into the Times , address to Mrs. Holbrook , from a relation ? she would be likely to answer that , when she would not reply to any appeal come directly from I . "

 CHAPTER XII 

 a FRIENDLY COUNSELLOR 


 Gilbert Fenton call several time in the Temple without be able to see John Saltram ; a slip of paper paste on the outer door of that gentleman 's chamber inform the public that he be " out of town , " and that be all .  Gilbert be inclined to believe that Jacob Nowell be a much rich man than he care to confess , and that the fortune which Marian Nowell might inherit in the future be a considerable one . the old man have all the attribute of a miser . the house in which he live have the aspect of a place in which money have be make and hoard day by day through long dull year .

 CHAPTER XIII 

 MRS . PALLINSON have view 


 at seven o'clock on Sunday evening , as the neighbouring church bell be just sound their last peal , Mr. Fenton find himself on the threshold of Mrs. Branston 's house in Cavendish - square . it be rather a gloomy mansion , pervade throughout with evidence of its late owner 's oriental career ; old indian cabinet ; ponderous chair of elaborately - carve ebony , clumsy in form and barbaric in design ; curious old china and lacquered ware of every kind , from gigantic vas to the tiny cup and saucer ; ivory temple , and god in silver and clay , crowd the drawing - room and the broad landing on the staircase . the curtain and chair - cover be of indian embroidery ; the carpet of oriental manufacture . everything have a gaudy semi - barbarous aspect .

 CHAPTER XV 

 on the TRACK

 there be time in which Marian Holbrook 's life would have be utterly lonely but for the companionship of Ellen Carley . this warm - hearted outspoken country girl have take a fancy to Mr. Holbrook 's beautiful wife from the hour of her arrival at the Grange , one cheerless March evening , and have attach herself to Marian from that moment with unalterable affection and fidelity . the girl 's own life at the Grange have be lonely enough , except during the brief summer month , when the roomy old house be now and then enliven a little by the advent of a lodger , some stray angler in search of a seclude trout stream , or an invalid who want quiet and fresh air . but in none of these stranger have Ellen ever take much interest . they have come and go , and make very little impression upon her mind , though she have help to make their sojourn pleasant in her own brisk cheery way . 

 she be twenty - one year of age , very bright - look , if not absolutely pretty , with dark expressive eye , a rosy brunette complexion , and very white tooth . the nose belong to the inferior order of pug or snub ; the forehead be low and broad , with dark - brown hair ripple over it hair which seem always want to escape from its neat arrangement into a multitude of mutinous curl . she be altogether a young person whom the admirer of the soubrette style of beauty might have find very charming ; and , seclude as her life at the Grange have be , she have already more than one admirer .

 CHAPTER xviii 

 JACOB NOWELL 's WILL 


 at his usual hour , upon the evening after his arrival in London , Gilbert Fenton call at the silversmith 's shop in Queen Anne 's Court . he find Jacob Nowell weak than when he have see he last , and with a strange old look , as if extreme age have come upon he suddenly . he have be compel to call in a medical man , very much against his will ; and this gentleman have tell he that his condition be a critical one , and that it would be well for he to arrange his affair quickly , and to hold himself prepared for the bad . 

 he seem to be slightly agitated when Gilbert tell he that his granddaughter have be find . 

 " will she come to I , do you think ? " he ask . 

 " I have no doubt that she will do so , directly she hear how ill you have be . she be very much pleased at the idea of see you , and only wait for her husband 's permission to come . but I do not   suppose she will wait for that when she know of your illness . I shall write to she immediately . "

" I do not   mind own to you that I have something considerable to bequeath ; for I think I can trust you . and if I should die before my grandchild come to I , you will see that she have her right , will not   you ? you will take care that she be not cheat by her husband , or by any one else ? "

"

 this be clear enough . it be only when there come the question , which a lawyer put with such deadly calmness , as to what be to be do with the money in the event of Marian Holbrook 's die intestate , that any perplexity arise . 

 " of course , if she have child , you would like the money to go to they , " say Mr. Medler , the attorney ; " that be clear enough , and have well be set out in your will . but suppose she should have no child , you would scarcely like all you leave to go to her husband , who be quite a stranger to you , and who may be a scoundrel for aught you know . " 

 " no ; I certainly should not   much care about enrich this Holbrook . " 

 " of course not ; to say nothing of the danger there would be in give he so strong an interest in his wife 's death . not but what I daresay he will contrive to squander the great part of the money during her lifetime . be it all in hard cash ? " 

 " no ; there be some house - property at Islington , which pay a high interest ; and there be other freehold . " 

 " then we might tie those up , give Mrs. Holbrook only the income . it be essential to provide against possible villany or extravagance on the part of the husband . woman be so weak and helpless in these matter . and in the event of your granddaughter die without child , would not   you rather let the estate go to your son ? "

 " that be a kind of oath which no man ever consider himself bind to keep , " say the lawyer in his most insinuating tone . " remember , it be only a remote contingency . the chance be that your granddaughter will have a family to inherit this property , and that she will survive her father . and then , if we give her power to make a will , of course it be pretty certain that she will leave everything to this husband of her . but I do not   think we ought to do that , Mr. Nowell . I think it would be a far wise arrangement to give this young lady only a life interest in the real estate . that make the husband a loser by her death , instead of a possible gainer to a large amount . and I consider that your son 's name have a right to come in here . "

 CHAPTER XIX 

 gilbert ask a QUESTION 


 Gilbert Fenton call at John Saltram 's chamber within a day or two of his return from Hampshire . he have a strange , almost feverish eagerness to see his old friend again ; a sense of have wrong he for that one brief moment of thought in which the possibility of his guilt have flash across his mind ; and with this feeling there be mingle a suspicion that John Saltram have not act quite fairly to he ; that he have keep back knowledge which must have come to he as an intimate ally of Sir David Forster .

 CHAPTER XX 

 drift AWAY

 CHAPTER XXI 

 FATHER and DAUGHTER

 CHAPTER XXII 

 AT LIDFORD AGAIN

Mr. Fenton have take life pleasantly enough , and yet have never spend five hundred a year . he could retire with an income of eight hundred and have abandon all idea of ever marry this seem to he more than sufficient .

 CHAPTER XXIII 

 call to ACCOUNT

 CHAPTER XXIV 

 TORMENTED by DOUBT 


 the long homeward walk give Gilbert ample leisure for reflection upon his interview with Sir David ; a very unsatisfactory interview at the good . yes , the conviction that the man who have wrong he be no other than his own familiar friend , have flash upon he with a new force as the Baronet answer his question about John Holbrook . the suspicion which have enter his mind after he leave the lonely farm - house near Crosber , and which he have do his uttermost to banish , as if it have be a suggestion of the evil one , come back to he to - day with a form and reality which it have lack before . it seem no long a vague fancy , a dark unwelcome thought that border on folly . it have take a new shape altogether , and appear to he almost a certainty .

Isabella be not a particularly mercenary person , but she be a woman of the world , and have an eye to the future aggrandisement of her child .
