 CHAPTER XXX 

 STRICKEN down 


 a hansom carry Gilbert Fenton to the Temple , without loss of time . there be a fierce hurry in his breast , a heat and fever which he have scarcely feel since the beginning of his trouble ; for his lurking suspicion of his friend have gather shape and strength all at once , and possess his mind now to the exclusion of every other thought . 

 he run quickly up the stair . the outer and inner door of John Saltram 's chamber be both ajar . Gilbert push they open and go in . the familiar sitting - room look just a little more dreary than usual . the litter of book and paper , ink - stand and portfolio , be transfer to one of the side - table , and in its place , on the table where his friend have be accustom to write , Gilbert see a cluster of medicine - bottle , a jug of toast - and - water , and a tray with a basin of lukewarm greasy - look beef - tea . 

 the door between the two room stand half open , and from the bedchamber within Gilbert hear the heavy painful breathing of a sleeper . he go to the door and look into the room . John Saltram be lie asleep , in an uneasy attitude , with both arm throw over his head . his face have a haggard look that be make all the more ghastly by two vivid crimson spot upon his sunken cheek ; there be dark purple ring round his eye , and his beard be of more than a week 's growth . 

 " Ill , " Gilbert mutter , look aghast at this dreary picture , with strangely conflict feeling of pity and anger in his breast ; ' strike down at the very moment when I have determine to know the truth . " 

 the sick man toss himself restlessly from side to side in his feverish sleep , change his position two or three time with evident weariness and pain , and then open his eye and star with a blank unseeing gaze at his friend . that look , without one ray of recognition , go to Gilbert 's heart somehow . 

 " o God , how fond I be of he ! " he say to himself . " and if he have be a traitor ! if he be to die like this , before I have wring the truth from he to die , and I not dare to cherish his memory to be oblige to live out my life with this doubt of he ! " 

 this doubt ! have he much reason to doubt two minute afterwards , when John Saltram raise himself on his gaunt arm , and look piteously round the room ? 

 " Marian ! " he call . " Marian ! " 

 " yes , " mutter Gilbert , " it be all true . he be call his wife . " 

 the revelation scarcely seem a surprise to he . little by little that suspicion , so vague and dim at first , have gather strength , and now that all his doubt receive confirmation from those unconscious lip , it seem to he as if he have know his friend 's falsehood for a long time . 

 " Marian , come here . come , child , come , " the sick man cry in feeble implore tone . " what , be you afraid of I ? be this death ? be I dead , and part from she ? would anything else keep she from I when I call for she , the poor child that love I so well ? and I have wish myself free of her God forgive I ! wish myself free . " 

 the word be mutter in broken gasp fragment of sentence ; but Gilbert hear they and understand they very easily . then , after look about the room , and look full at Gilbert without see he , John Saltram fall back upon his tumble pillow and close his eye . Gilbert hear a slipshod step in the outer room , and turn round , find himself face to face with the laundress that mature and somewhat depressing matron whom he have seek out a little time before , when he want to discover Mr. Saltram 's whereabout . 

 this woman , upon see he , burst forth immediately into jubilation . 

 " o , sir , what a providence it be that you have   come ! " she cry . " poor dear gentleman , he have be that ill , and I not know what to do more than a baby , except in the way of send for a doctor when I see how bad he be , and wait on he myself day and night , which I have do faithful , and be that wear - out in consequence , that I shake like a haspen , and can not   touch a bit of victual . I have but just slip round to the court , while he be asleep , poor dear , to give my child their dinner ; for it be a hard trial , sir , have a helpless young family depend upon one ; and it would but be fair that all I have go through should be consider ; for though I say it as should not , there be not   one of your hire nurse would do more ; and I be willing to continue of it , provisoe as I have help at night , and my trouble consider in my wage . " 

 " you need have no apprehension ; you shall be pay for your trouble . have he be long ill ? " 

 " well , sir , he take the cold as be the beginning of his illness a fortnight ago come next Thursday . you may remember , perhaps , as it come on awful wet in the afternoon , last Thursday week , and Mr. Saltram be out in the rain , and walk home in it , not be able to get a cab , I suppose , or perhaps not care to get one , for he be always a careless gentleman in such respect , and come in wet through to the skin ; and instead of change his clothe , as a Christian would have do , just give himself a shake like , as he might have be a New - fondling dog that have be swim , and sit down before the fire , which of course draw out the steam from his thing and make it bad , and write away for dear life till twelve o'clock that night , have something particular to finish for they magazine , he say ; and so , when I come to tidy - up a bit the last thing at night , I find he sit at the table writing , and do not   take no more notice of I than a dog , which be his way , though never mean unkindly quite the reverse . " 

 the laundress pause to draw breath , and to pour a dose of medicine from one of the bottle on the table . 

 " well , sir , the next day , he have a vi'lent cold , as you may suppose , and be low and languid - like , but go on with his writing , and it be not   no good ask he not . ' I want money , Mrs. Pratt , ' he say ; ' you can not   tell how bad I want money , and these people pay I for my stuff as fast as I send it in . ' the day after that he be a deal bad , and have a wandering way like , as if he do not   know what he be do ; and sit turn over his paper with one hand , and lean his head upon the other , and groan so that it go through one like a knife to hear he . ' it be no use , ' he say at last ; ' it be no use ! ' and then go and throw hisself down upon that bed , and have never get up since , poor dear gentleman ! I go round to fetch a doctor out of Essex Street , find as he be no well in the evening , and awful hot , and still more wandering - like Mr. Mew by name , a very nice gentleman which say as it be rheumatic fever , and have be here twice a day ever since . " 

 " have Mr. Saltram never be in his right sens since that day ? " Gilbert ask . 

 " o yes , sir ; off and on for the first week he be quite hisself at time ; but for the last three day he have not   know any one , and have talk and jabber a deal , and have be dreadful restless . " 

 " do the doctor call it a dangerous case ? " 

 " well , sir , not to deceive you , he ast I if Mr. Saltram have any friend as I could send for ; and I say no , not to my knowledge ; ' for , ' say Mr. Mew , ' if he have any relation or friend near at hand , they ought to be tell that he be in a bad way ; ' and only this morning he say as how he should like to call in a physician , for the case be a bad one . " 

 " I see . there be danger evidently , " Gilbert say gravely . " I will wait and hear what the doctor say . he will come again to - day , I suppose ? " 

 " yes , sir ; he be sure to come in the evening . " 

 " good ; I will stay till the evening . I should like you to go round immediately to this Mr. Mew 's house , and ask for the address of some skilled nurse , and then go on , in a cab if necessary , and fetch she . " 

 " I could do that , sir , of course , not but what I feel myself capable of nurse the poor dear gentleman . " 

 " you can not   nurse he night and day , my good woman . do what I tell you , and bring back a professional nurse as soon as you can . if Mr. Mew should be out , his people be likely to know the address of such a person . " 

 he give the woman some silver , and despatch she ; and then , be alone , sit down quietly in the sick - room to think out the situation . 

 yes , there be no long any doubt ; that piteous appeal to Marian have settle the question . John Saltram , the friend whom he have love , be the traitor . John Saltram have steal his promise wife , have come between he and his fair happy future , and have keep the secret of his guilt in a dastardly spirit that make the act fifty time black than it would have seem otherwise . 

 sit in the dreary silence of that sick chamber , a silence break only by the painful sound of the sleeper 's difficult breathing , many thing come back to his mind ; circumstance trivial enough in themselves , but invest with a grave significance when contemplate by the light of today 's revelation . 

 he remember those happy autumn afternoon at Lidford ; those long , drowsy , idle day in which John Saltram have give himself up so entirely to the pleasure of the moment , with surely something more than mere sympathy with his friend 's happiness . he remember that last long evening at the cottage , when this man have be at his good , full of life and gaiety ; and then that sudden departure , which have puzzle he so much at the time , and yet have seem no surprise to Marian . it have be the result of some suddenly - form resolution perhaps , Gilbert think . 

 " poor wretch ! he may have try to be true to I , " he say to himself , with a sharp bitter pain at his heart . 

 he have love this man so well , that even now , know himself to have be betray , there be a strange mingling of pity and anger in his mind , and mix with these a touch of contempt . he have believe in John Saltram ; have fancy he nobler and grander than himself , somehow ; a man who , under a careless half - scornful pretence of be bad than his fellow , conceal a nature that be far above the common herd ; and yet this man have prove the mere caitiff , a weak cowardly villain . 

 " to take my hand in friendship , know what he have do , and how my life be break ! to pretend sympathy ; to play out the miserable farce to the very last ! great heaven ! that the man I have honour could be capable of so much baseness ! " 

 the sleeper move restlessly , the eye be open once more and turn upon Gilbert , not with the same utter blankness as before , but without the faint recognition . the sick man see some one watch he , and the figure be associate with an unreal presence , the phantom of his brain , which have be with he often in the day and night . 

 " the man again ! " he mutter . " when will she come ? " and then raise himself upon his elbow , he cry imploringly , " Mother , you fetch she ! " 

 he be speak to his mother , whom he have love very dearly his mother who have be dead fifteen year . 

 Gilbert 's mind go back to that far - away time in Egypt , when he have lain like this , helpless and unconscious , and this man have nurse and watch he with unwearye tenderness . 

 " I will see he safely through this , " he say to himself , " and then " 

 and then the account between they must be square somehow . Gilbert Fenton have no thought of any direful vengeance . he belong to an age in which injury be take very quietly , unless they be wrong which the law can redress wound which can be heal by a golden plaster in the way of damage . 

 he could not kill his friend ; the age of duelling be past , and he not romantic enough to be guilty of such an anachronism as mortal combat . yet nothing less than a duel to the death could avenge such a wrong . 

 so friendship be at an end between those two , and that be all ; it be only the utter severance of a tie that have last for year , nothing more . yet to Gilbert it seem a great deal . his little world have crumble to ash ; love have perish , and now friendship have die this sudden bitter death , from which there be no possible resurrection . 

 in the midst of such thought as these he remember the sick man 's medicine . Mrs. Pratt have give he a few hurried direction before depart on her errand . he look at his watch , and then go over to the table and prepare the draught and administer it with a firm and gentle hand . 

 " who be that ? " John Saltram mutter faintly . " it seem like the touch of a friend . " 

 he drop back upon the pillow without wait for any reply , and fall into a string of low incoherent talk , with closed eye . 

 the laundress be a long time go , and Gilbert sit alone in the dismal little bedroom , where there have never be the small attempt at comfort since John Saltram have occupy it . he sit alone , or with that awful companionship of one whose mind be far away , which be so much more dreary than actual loneliness sit brood over the history of his friend 's treachery . 

 what have he do with Marian ? be her disappearance any work of his , after all ? have he hide she away for some secret reason of his own , and then act out the play by pretend to search for she ? know he for the traitor he be , could Gilbert Fenton draw any positive line of demarcation between the amount of guilt which be possible and that which be not possible to he ? 

 what have he do with Marian ? how soon would he be able to answer that question ? or would he ever be able to answer it ? the thought of this delay be torture to Gilbert Fenton . he have come here to - day thinking to make an end of all his doubt , to force an avowal of the truth from those false lip . and behold , a hand strong than his hold he back . his interrogation must await the answer to that awful question life or death . 

 the woman come in presently , bustling and out of breath . she have find a very trustworthy person , recommend by Mr. Mew 's assistant a person who would come that evening without fail . 

 " it be all the way up at Islington , sir , and I pay the cabman three - and - six altogether , which he say it be his fare . and how have the poor dear been while I be away ? " ask Mrs. Pratt , with her head on one side and an air of extreme solicitude . 

 " very much as you see he now . he have mention a name once or twice , the name of Marian . have you ever hear that ? " 

 " I should say I have , sir , time and often since he be be ill . ' Marian , why do not   you come to I ? ' so pitiful ; and then , ' lost , lose ! ' in such a awful wild way . I think it must be some favourite sister , sir , or a young lady as he have kep ' company with . " 

 " Marian ! " cry the voice from the bed , as if their cautious talk have penetrate to that dim brain . " marian ! o no , no ; she be go ; I have lose she ! well , I wish it ; I want my freedom . " 

 Gilbert start , and stand transfix , look intently at the unconscious speaker . yes , here be the clue to the mystery . John Saltram have grow tired of his steal bride have sigh for his freedom . who should say that he have not take some iniquitous mean to rid himself of the tie that have grow troublesome to he ? 

 Gilbert Fenton remember Ellen Carley 's suspicion . he be no long incline to despise they . 

 it be dreary work to sit by the bedside watch that familiar face , to which fever and delirium have give a strange weird look ; dismal work to count the moment , and wonder when that voice , now so thick of utterance as it go on mutter incoherent sentence and meaningless phrase , would be able to reply to those question which Gilbert Fenton be burn to ask . 

 be it a guilty conscience , the dull slow agony of remorse , which have stricken this man down this strong powerfully - build man , who be a stranger to illness and all physical suffering ? be the body only crush by the burden of the mind ? Gilbert could not find any answer to these question . he only know that his sometime friend lie there helpless , unconscious , remove beyond his reach as completely as if he have be lie in his coffin . 

 " o God , it be hard to bear ! " he say half aloud : " it be a bitter trial to bear . if this illness should end in death , I may never know Marian 's fate . " 

 he sit in the sick man 's room all through that long dismal afternoon , wait to see the doctor , and with the same hopeless thought repeat themselves perpetually in his mind . 

 it be nearly eight o'clock when Mr. Mew at last make his evening visit . he be a grave gray - haired little man , with a shrewd face and a pleasant manner ; a man who inspire Gilbert with confidence , and whose presence be cheer in a sick - room ; but he do not speak very hopefully of John Saltram . 

 " it be a bad case , sir a very bad case , " he say gravely , after he have make his careful examination of the patient 's condition . " there have be a violent cold catch , you see , through our poor friend 's recklessness in neglect to change his damp clothe , and rheumatic fever have set in . but it appear to I that there be other cause at work mental disturbance , and so on . our friend have be tax his brain a little too severely , I gather from Mrs. Pratt 's account of he ; and these thing will tell , sir ; soon or later they have their effect . " 

 " then you apprehend danger ? " 

 " well , yes ; I dare not tell you that there be an absence of danger . Mr. Saltram have a fine constitution , a noble frame ; but the strain be a severe one , especially upon the mind . " 

 " you speak just now of over - work as a cause for this mental disturbance . might it not rather proceed from some secret trouble of mind , some hide care ? " Gilbert ask anxiously . 

 " that , sir , be an open question . the mind be unhinge ; there be no doubt of that . there be something more here than the ordinary delirium we look for in fever case . " 

 " you have talk of a physician , Mr. Mew ; would it not be well to call one in immediately ? " 

 " I should feel more comfortable if my opinion be support , sir : not that I believe there be anything more can be do for our patient than I have be do ; but the case be a critical one , and I should be glad to feel myself support . " 

 " if you will give I the name and address of the gentleman you would like to call in , I will go for he immediately . " 

 " to - night ? Nay , my dear sir , there be no occasion for such haste ; to - morrow morning will do very well . " 

 " to - morrow morning , then ; but I will make the appointment to - night , if I can . " 

 Mr. Mew name a physician high in reputation as a specialist in such case as John Saltram 's ; and Gilbert dash off at once in a hansom to obtain the promise of an early visit from this gentleman on the following morning . he succeed in his errand ; and on return to the Temple find the professional nurse instal , and the sick - room brighten and freshen a little by her handiwork . the patient be asleep , and his slumber be more quiet than usual . 

 Gilbert have eat nothing since breakfast , and it be now nearly nine o'clock in the evening ; but before go out to some neighbour tavern to snatch a hasty dinner , he stop to tell Mrs. Pratt that he should sleep in his friend 's chamber that night . 

 " why , you do not   mean that , sir , sure to goodness , " cry the laundress , alarmed ; " and not so much as a sofy bedstead , nor nothing anyway comfortable . " 

 " I could sleep upon three or four chair , if it be necessary ; but there be an old sofa in the bedroom . you might bring that into this room for I ; and the nurse can have it in the day - time . she will not   want to be lie down to - night , I daresay . I do not   suppose I shall sleep much myself , but I be a little knock up , and shall be glad of some sort of rest . I want to be on the spot , come what may . " 

 " but , sir , with the new nurse and I , there surely can not   be no necessity ; and you might be round the first thing in the morning like to see how the poor dear gentleman have slep ' . " 

 " I know that , but I would rather be on the spot . I have my own especial reason . you can go home to your child . " 

 " thank you kindly , sir ; which I shall be very glad to take care of them , poor thing . and I hope , sir , as you will not   forget that I have   go through a deal for Mr. Saltram if so be as he should not   get well himself , which the Lord forbid to take my trouble into consideration , bein ' as he be always a free - handed gentleman , though not rich . " 

 " your service will not be forget , Mrs. Pratt , depend upon it . perhaps I would well give you a couple of sovereign on account : that will make matter straight for the present . " 

 " yes , sir ; and many thank for your generosity , " reply the laundress , agreeably surprise by this prompt donation , and drop grateful curtsey before her benefactor ; " and Mr. Saltram shall want nothing as my care can provide for he , you may depend upon it . " 

 " that be well . and now I be go out to get some dinner ; I shall be back in half an hour . " 

 the press and bustle of the day 's work be over at the tavern to which Gilbert bend his step . dinner and diner seem to be do with for one more day ; and there be only a couple of drowsy - look waiter fold table - cloth and put away cruet - stand and other paraphernalia in long narrow closet cut in the paper wall , and invisible by day . 

 one of these functionary grow brisk again , with a wan factitious briskness , at sight of Gilbert , make haste to redecorate one of the table , and in bland insinuate tone suggest a dinner of six course or so , as likely to be agreeable to a lonely and belate diner ; well aware in the depth of his inner consciousness that the six course would be all more or less warming - up of viand that have figure in the day 's bill of fare . 

 " bring I a chop or a steak , and a pint of dry sherry , " Gilbert say wearily . 

 " have a slice of turbot and lobster - sauce , sir the turbot be uncommon fine to - day ; and a briled fowl and mushroom . it will be ready in five minute . " 

 " you may bring I the fowl , if you like : I will not   wait for fish . I be in a hurry . " 

 the attendant give a faint sigh , and communicate the order for the fowl and mushroom through a speaking - tube . it be the business of his life to beguile his master 's customer into over - eat themselves , and to set his face against chop and steak ; but he feel that this particular customer be proof against his blandishment . he take Gilbert an evening paper , and then subside into a pensive silence until the fowl appear in an agreeable frizzling state , fresh from the gridiron , but a bird of some experience notwithstanding , and wingless . it be a very hasty meal . Gilbert be eager to return to those chamber in the Temple eager to be listen once more for some chance word of meaning that might be drop from John Saltram 's pale parch lip in the midst of incoherent raving . come what might , he want to be near at hand , to watch that sick - bed with a close vigil than hire nurse ever keep ; to be ready to surprise the brief interval of consciousness that might come all of a sudden to that hapless fever - stricken sinner . who should say that such an interval would not come , or who could tell what such an interval might reveal ? 

 Gilbert Fenton pay for his dinner , leave half his wine undrunk , and hurry away ; leave the waiter with rather a contemptuous idea of he , though that individual condescend to profit by his sobriety , and finish the dry sherry at a draught . 

 it be nearly ten when Gilbert return to the chamber , and all be still quiet , that heavy slumber continue ; an artificial sleep at the good , produce by one of Mr. Mew 's sedative . the sofa have be wheel from the bedroom to the sitting - room , and place in a comfortable corner by the fire . there be preparation too for a cup of tea , to be make and consume at any hour agreeable to the watcher ; a small teakettle simmer on the hob ; a tray with a cup and saucer , and queer little black earthenware teapot , on the table ; a teacaddy and other appliance close at hand , all testify to the grateful attention of the vanish Pratt . 

 Gilbert share the nurse 's watch till past midnight . long before that John Saltram wake from his heavy sleep , and there be more of that incoherent talk so painful to hear talk of people that be dead , of scene that be far away , even of those careless happy wandering in which those two college friend have be together ; and then mere nonsense talk , shred and patch of random thought , that scorn to be draw from some rubbish - chamber , some waste - paper basket of the brain . 

 it be weary work . he wake towards eleven , and a little after twelve drop asleep again ; but this time , the effect of the sedative have wear off , the sleep be restless and uneasy . then come a brief interval of quiet ; and in this Gilbert leave he , and fling himself down upon the sofa , to sink into a slumber that be scarcely more peaceful than that of the sick man . 

 he be thoroughly wear out , however , and sleep for some hour , to be awaken suddenly at last by a shrill cry in the next room . he spring up from the sofa , and rush in . John Saltram be sit up in bed , prop by the pillow on which his two elbow be plant , look about he with a fierce haggard face , and call for " Marian . " the nurse have fall asleep in her arm - chair by the fire , and be slumber placidly . 

 " Marian , " he cry , " Marian , why have you leave I ? God know I love you ; yes , even when I seem cold and neglectful . everything be against I ; but I love you , my dear , I love you ! do I ever say that you come between I and fortune be I mean enough , base enough , ever to say that ? it be a lie , my love ; you be my fortune . be poverty and obscurity hard thing to bear for you ? no , my darling , no ; I will face they to - morrow , if you will come back to I . o no , no , she be go ; my life have go : I break her heart with my hard bitter word ; I drive my angel away from I . " 

 he have not speak so coherently since Gilbert have be with he that day . surely this must be an interval of consciousness , or semi - consciousness . Gilbert go to the bedside , and , seat himself there quietly , look intently at the altered face , which star at he without a gleam of recognition . 

 " speak to I , John Saltram , " he say . " you know I , do not   you the man who be once your friend , Gilbert Fenton ? " 

 the other burst into a wild bitter laugh . " Gilbert Fenton my friend , the man who trust I still ! poor old Gilbert ! and I fancy that I love he , that I would have freely sacrifice my own happiness for his . " 

 " and yet you betray he , " Gilbert say in a low distinct voice . " but that may be forgive , if you have be guilty of no deeply wrong than that . John Saltram , as you have a soul to be save , what have you do with Marian with your wife ? " 

 it cost he something , even in that moment of excitement , to pronounce those two word . 

 " kill she ! " the sick man answer with the same mad laugh . " she be too good for I , you see ; and I grow weary of her calm beauty , and I sicken of her tranquil goodness . first I sacrifice honour , friendship , everything to win she ; and then I get tired of my prize . it be my nature , I suppose ; but I love she all the time ; she have twin herself about my heart somehow . I know it when she be lose . " 

 " what have you do with she ? " repeat Gilbert , in a low stern voice , with his grasp upon John Saltram 's arm . 

 " what have I do with she ? I forget . she be go I want my freedom ; I feel myself fetter , a ruined man . she be go ; and I be free , free to make a well marriage . " 

 " o God ! " mutter Gilbert , " be this man the black villain that ever cumber the earth ? what be I to think , what be I to believe ? " 

 again he repeat the same question , with a stern kind of patience , as if he would give this guilty wretch the benefit of every possible doubt , the unwilling pity which his condition demand . alas ! he could obtain no coherent answer to his persistent questioning . Vague self - accusation , mad reiteration of that one fact of his loss ; nothing more distinct come from those fevere lip , nor do one look of recognition flash into those bloodshot eye . 

 the time at which this mystery be to be solve have not come yet ; there be nothing to be do but to wait , and Gilbert wait with a sublime patience through all the alternation of a long and wearisome sickness . 

 " talk of friend , " Mrs. Pratt exclaim , in a private conference with the nurse ; " never do I see such a friend as Mr. Fenting , sacrifice of himself as he do , day and night , to look after that poor creature in there , and take no well rest than he can get on that old horsehair sofy , which brickbat or knife board be not   hard , and never do you hear he murmur . " 

 and yet for this man , whose battle with the grim enemy , death , he watch so patiently , what feeling could there be in Gilbert Fenton 's heart in all the day to come but hatred or contempt ? he have love he so well , and trust he so completely , and this be the end of it . 

 Christmas come while John Saltram be lie at death 's door , feebly fight that awful battle , struggle unconsciously with the bony hand that be try to drag he across that fatal threshold ; just able to keep himself on this side of that dread portal beyond which there lie so deep a mystery , so profound a darkness . Christmas come ; and there be bell ring , and festive gathering here and there about the great dreary town , and Gilbert Fenton be besiege by friendly invitation from Mrs. Lister , remonstrate with he for his want of common affection in prefer to spend that season among his London friend rather than in the bosom of his family . 

 Gilbert write : to his sister tell she that he have particular business which detain he in town . but have it be otherwise , have he not be bind prisoner to John Saltram 's sick - room , he would scarcely have care to take his part in the conventional feasting and commonplace joviality of Lidford House . have he not dream of a bright home which be to be his at this time , a home beautify by the presence of the woman he love ? ah , what delight to have welcome the sacred day in the holy quiet of such a home , they two alone together , with all the world shut out ! 

 CHAPTER XXXI 

 ELLEN CARLEY 's TRIALS 


 Christmas come in the old farm - house near Crosber ; and Ellen Carley , who have no idea of make any troubled thought of her own an excuse for neglect of her household duty , make the sombre panel room bright with holly and ivy , laurel and fir , and busy herself briskly in the confection of such pie and pudding as Hampshire consider necessary to the due honour of that pious festival . there be not many people to see the greenery and bright holly - berry which embellish the grave old room , not many whom Ellen very much care for to taste the pie and pudding ; but duty must be do , and the bailiff 's daughter do she work with a steady industry which know no wavering . 

 her life have be a hard one of late , very lonely since Mrs. Holbrook 's disappearance , and haunt with a presence which be most hateful to she . Stephen Whitelaw have take to come to the Grange much oftener than of old . there be seldom an evening now on which his insignificant figure be not to be see plant by the hearth in the snug little oak - parlour , smoke his pipe in that dull silent way of his , which be calculate to aggravate a lively person like Ellen Carley into some open expression of disgust or dislike . of late , too , his attention have be of a more pronounced character ; he take to drop sly hint of his pretension , and it be impossible for Ellen any long to doubt that he want she to be his wife . More than this , there be a tone of assurance about the man , quiet as he be , which exasperate Miss Carley beyond all measure . he have the air of be certain of success , and on more than one occasion speak of the day when Ellen would be mistress of Wyncomb Farm . 

 on his repetition of this offensive speech one evening , the girl take he up sharply : 

 " not quite so fast , if you please , Mr. Whitelaw , " she say ; " it take two to make a bargain of that kind , just the same as it take two to quarrel . there be many curious change may come in a person 's life , no doubt , and folk never know what be go to happen to they ; but whatever change may come upon I , that be not   one of they . I may live to see the inside of the workhouse , perhaps , when I be too old for service ; but I shall never sleep under the roof of Wyncomb Farmhouse . " 

 Mr. Whitelaw give a spiteful little laugh . 

 " what a spirited one she be , be not   she , now ? " he say with a sneer . " o , you will not , will not   you , my lass ; you turn up that pretty little nose of yours it do turn up a bit of itself , do not   it , though ? at Wyncomb Farm and Stephen Whitelaw ; your father tell a different story , Nell . " 

 " then my father tell a lie story , " answer the girl , blush crimson with indignation ; " and it be not   for want o ' know the truth . he know that , if it be put upon I to choose between your house and the union , I would go to the union and with a light heart too , to be free of you . I do not   want to be rude , Mr. Whitelaw ; for you have   be civil - speak enough to I , and I daresay you be a good friend to my father ; but I can not   help speak the truth , and you have   bring it on yourself with your nonsense . " 

 " she be get a devil of a tongue of her own , you see , Whitelaw , " say the bailiff , with a savage glance at his daughter ; " but she do not   mean above a quarter what she say and when her time come , she will do as she be bid , or she be no child of mine . " 

 " o , I forgive she , " reply Mr. Whitelaw , with a placid air of superiority ; " I be not the man to bear malice against a pretty woman , and to my mind a pretty woman look all the pretty when she be in a passion . I be not in a hurry , you see , Carley ; I can bide my time ; but I shall never take a mistress to Wyncomb unless I can take the one I like . " 

 after this particular evening , Mr. Whitelaw 's presence seem more than ever disagreeable to poor Ellen . he have the air of her fate somehow , sit root to the hearth night after night , and she grow to regard he with a half superstitious horror , as if he possess some occult power over she , and could bend she to his wish in spite of herself . the very quietude of the man become appalling to she . such a man seem capable of accomplish anything by the mere force of persistence , by the negative power that lie in his silent nature . 

 " I suppose he mean to sit in that room night after night , smoke his pipe and star with those pale stupid eye of his , till I change my mind and promise to marry he , " Ellen say to herself , as she meditate angrily on the annoyance of Mr. Whitelaw 's courtship . " he may sit there till his hair turn gray if ever such red hair do turn to anything well than itself and he will find no change in I . I wish Frank be here to keep up my courage . I think if he be to ask I to run away with he , I should be tempt to say yes , at the risk of bring ruin upon both of we ; anything to escape out of the power of that man . but come what may , I will not   endure it much long . I will run away to service soon after Christmas , and father will only have himself to thank for the loss of I . " 

 it be Mr. Whitelaw who appear as principal guest at the Grange on Christmas - day ; Mr. Whitelaw , support on this occasion by a widow cousin of his who have keep house for he for some year , and who bear a strong family likeness to he both in person and manner , and Ellen Carley think that it be impossible for the world to contain a more disagreeable pair . these be the guest who consume great quantity of Ellen 's pie and pudding , and who sit under her festal garland of holly and laurel . she have be especially careful to hang no scrap of mistletoe , which might have afford Mr. Whitelaw an excuse for a practical display of his gallantry ; a fact which do not escape the playful observation of his cousin , Mrs. Tadman . 

 " young lady do not   often forget to put up a bit of mistletoe , " say this matron , " when there be a chance of they they like be by ; " and she glance in a meaning way from Ellen to the master of Wyncomb Farm . 

 " Miss Carley be not   like the generality of young lady , " Mr. Whitelaw answer with a glum look , and his kinswoman be fain to drop the subject . 

 alone with Ellen , sly Mrs. Tadman take occasion to launch out into enthusiastic praise of her cousin ; to which the girl listen in profound silence , closely watch all the time by the woman 's sharp gray eye . and then by degree her tone change ever so little , and she own that her kinsman be not altogether faultless ; indeed it be curious to perceive what numerous shortcoming be coexistent with those shin merit of his . 

 " he have be a good friend to I , " continue the matron ; " that I never have deny and never shall deny . but I have be a good servant to he ; ah ! there be not   a hire servant as would toil and drudge , and watch and pinch , as I have do to please he , and never have have payment from he more than a new gown at Christmas , or a five - pound note after harvest . and of course , if ever he marry , I shall have to look for a new home ; for I know too much of his way , I daresay , for a wife to like to have I about she and I of an age when it seem a hard to have to go among stranger and not have save sixpence , where I might have put by a hundred pound easy , if I have not   be work without wage for a relation . but I have   not be call a servant , you see ; and I suppose Stephen think that be payment enough for my trouble . goodness know I have   save he many a pound , and that he will know when I be go ; for he be near , be Stephen , and it go to his heart to part with a shilling . " 

 " but why should you ever leave he , Mrs. Tadman ? " Ellen ask kindly . " I should not   think he could have a well housekeeper . " 

 " perhaps not , " answer the widow , shake her head with mysterious significance ; " but his wife will not   think that ; and when he be get a wife he will want she to be his housekeeper , and to pinch and scrape as I have   pinch and scrap for he . Lord help she ! " conclude Mrs. Tadman , with a faint groan , which be far from complimentary to her relative 's character . 

 " but perhaps he never will marry , " argue Ellen coolly . 

 " o , yes , he will , Miss Carley , " reply Mrs. Tadman , with another significant movement of her head ; " he be set his heart on that , and he be set his heart on the young woman he mean to marry . " 

 " he can not   marry she unless she be willing to be his wife , any how , " say Ellen , redden a little . 

 " o , he will find a way to make her consent , Miss Carley , depend upon that . whatever Stephen Whitelaw set his mind upon , he will do . but I do not   envy that poor young woman ; for she will have a hard life of it at Wyncomb , and a hard master in my cousin Stephen . " 

 " she must be a very weak - minded young woman if she marry he against her will , " Ellen say laugh ; and then run off to get the tea ready , leave Mrs. Tadman to her meditation , which be not of a lively nature at the good of time . 

 that Christmas - day come to an end at last , after a long evening in the oak parlour enliven by a solemn game at whist and a ponderous supper of cold sirloin and mince pie ; and look out at the wintry moonlight , and the shadowy garden and flat waste of farm - land from the narrow casement in her own room . Ellen Carley wonder what those she love well in the world be do and think of under that moonlit sky . where be Marian Holbrook , that new - find friend whom she have love so well , and whose fate remain so profound a mystery ? and what be Frank Randall do , far away in London , where he have go to fill a responsible position in a large City firm of solicitor , and whence he have promise to return faithful to his first love , as soon as he find himself fairly on the road to a competence wherewith to endow she ? 

 thus it be that poor Ellen keep the close of her Christmas - day , look out over the cold moonlit field , and wonder how she be to escape from the persecution of Stephen Whitelaw . 

 that obnoxious individual have invite Mr. Carley and his daughter to spend New - year's - day at Wyncomb ; a display of hospitality so foreign to his character , that it be scarcely strange that Mrs. Tadman open her eye and star aghast as she hear the invitation give . it have be accept too , much to Ellen 's disgust ; and her father tell she more than once in the course of the ensue week that she be to put on her good gown , and smarten herself up a bit , on New - year's - day . 

 " and if you want a new gown , Nell , I do not   mind give it you , " say the bailiff , in a burst of generosity , and with the prevail masculine idea that a new gown be a panacea for all feminine grief . " you can walk over to Malsham and buy it any afternoon you like . " 

 but Ellen do not care for a new gown , and tell her father so , with a word or two of thank for his offer . she do not desire fine dress ; she have indeed be look over and furbish up her wardrobe of late , with a view to that possible flight of her , and it be to her cotton working gown that she have pay most attention : look forward to begin a hard life in some stranger 's service ready to endure anything rather than to marry Stephen Whitelaw . and of late the conviction have grow upon she that her father be very much in earnest , and that before long it would be a question whether she should obey he , or be turn out of door . she have see his dealing with other people , and she know he to be a passionate determine man , hard as iron in his anger . 

 " I will not   give he the trouble to turn I out of door , " Ellen say to herself . " when I know his mind , and that there be no hope of turn he , I will get away quietly , and find some new home . he have no real power over I , and I have but to earn my own living to be independent of he . and I do not   suppose Frank will think any the bad of I for have be a servant , " think the girl , with something like a sob . it seem hard that she must need sink lower in her lover 's eye , when she be so far beneath he already ; he a lawyer 's son , a gentleman by education , and she an untaught country girl . 

 CHAPTER XXXII 

 the padlocked door AT WYNCOMB 


 the countenance of the new year be harsh , rugged , and gloomy as of a stony - hearted , strong - minded new year , that have no idea of make his wintry aspect pleasant , or brighten the gloom of his infancy with any deceptive gleam of January sunshine . a bitter north wind make a dreary howling among the leafless tree , and sweep across the broad bare field with merciless force a bleak cruel new - year's - day , on which to go out a - pleasuring ; but it be more in harmony with Ellen Carley 's thought than bright weather could have be ; and she go to and fro about her morning 's work , up and down cold windy passage , and in and out of the frozen dairy , unmove by the bitter wind which sweep the crisp wave of dark brown hair from her low brow , and ting the tip of her impertinent little nose with a faint wintry bloom . 

 the bailiff be in very high spirit this first morning of the new year almost uproarious spirit indeed , which vent themselves in snatch of boisterous song , as he bustle backwards and forwards from house to stable , dress in his good blue coat and bright button and a capacious buff waistcoat ; with his ponderous nether limb clothe in knee - cord , and boot with vinegar top ; look altogether the typical british farmer . 

 those riotous burst of song make his daughter shudder . somehow , his gaiety be more alarming to she than his customary morose humour . it be all the more singular , too , because of late William Carley have be especially silent and moody , with the air of a man whose mind be weigh down by some heavy burden so gloomy indeed , that his daughter have question he more than once , entreat to know if he be distress by any secret trouble , anything go wrong about the farm , and so on . the girl have only bring upon herself harsh angry answer by these considerate inquiry , and have be tell to mind her own business , and not pry into matter that in no way concern she . 

 " but it do concern I to see you downhearte , father , " she answer gently . 

 " do it really , my girl ? what ! your father 's something more than a stranger to you , be he ? I should not   have think it , see how you have   go again I in some thing lately . Howsomedever , when I want your help , I shall know how to ask for it , and I hope you will give it freely . I do not   want fine word ; they never pull anybody out of the ditch that I have   hear tell of . " 

 whatever the bailiff 's trouble have be , it seem to be lighten to - day , Ellen think ; and yet that unusual noisy gaiety of his give she an uncomfortable feeling : it do not seem natural or easy . 

 her household work be do by noon , and she dress hurriedly , while her father call for she impatiently from below stand at the foot of the wide bare old staircase , and bawl up to she that they should be late at Wyncomb . she look very pretty in her neat dark - blue merino dress and plain linen collar , when she come trip downstairs at last , flush with the hurry of her toilet , and altogether so bright a creature that it seem a hard thing she should not be set out upon some real pleasure trip , instead of that most obnoxious festival to which she be summon . 

 her father look at she with a grim kind of approval . 

 " you will do well enough , lass , " he say ; " but I should like you to have have something smart than that blue stuff . I would not   have mind a couple of pound or so to buy you a silk gown . but you will be able to buy yourself as many silk gown as ever you like by - and - by , if you play your card well and do not   make a fool of yourself . " 

 Ellen know what he mean well enough , but do not care to take any notice of the speech . the time would soon come , no doubt , when she must take her stand in direct opposition to he , and in the meanwhile it would be bad than foolish to waste breath in idle squabbling . 

 they be to drive to Wyncomb in the bailiff 's gig ; rather an obsolete vehicle , with a yellow body , a mouldy leather apron , and high wheel pick out with red , draw by a tall gray horse that do duty with the plough on ordinary occasion . Stephen Whitelaw 's house be within an easy walk of the Grange ; but the gig be a more dignified mode of approach than a walk , and the bailiff insist on drive his daughter to her suitor 's abode in that conveyance . 

 Wyncomb be a long low gray stone house , of an unknown age ; a spacious habitation enough , with many room , and no less than three staircase , but possess no trace of that fall grandeur which pervade the Grange . it have be nothing well than a farm - house from time immemorial , and have be add to and extend and alter to suit the convenience of successive generation of farmer . it be a gloomy - look house at all time , Ellen Carley think , but especially gloomy under that leaden winter sky ; a house which it would have be almost impossible to associate with pleasant family gathering or the joyous voice of young child ; a grim desolate - look house , that seem to freeze the pass traveller with its cold blank stare , as if its gloomy portal have a voice to say to he , " however lose you may be for lack of shelter , however weary for want of rest , come not here ! " 

 idle fancy , perhaps ; but they be the thought with which Wyncomb Farmhouse always inspire Ellen Carley . 

 " the place just suit its master 's hard miserly nature , " she say . " one would think it have be make on purpose for he ; or perhaps the Whitelaws have be like that from generation to generation . " 

 there be no such useless adornment as a flower - garden at Wyncomb . Stephen Whitelaw care about as much for rose and lily as he care for greek poetry or Beethoven 's sonata . at the back of the house there be a great patch of bare shadowless ground devote to cabbage and potato , with a straggling border of savoury herb ; a patch not even divide from the farm land beyond , but melt imperceptibly into a field of mangel - wurzel . there be no superfluous hedge upon Mr. Whitelaw 's dominion ; not a solitary tree to give shelter to the tired cattle in the long hot summer day . noble old oak and patriarch beech , tall sycamore and grand flowering chestnut , have be stub up remorselessly by that economical agriculturist ; and he be now the proud possessor of one of the ugly and most profitable farm in Hampshire . 

 in front of the gray - stone house the sheep browse up to the parlour window , and on both side of the ill - keep carriage - drive lead from the white gate that open into the meadow to the door of Mr. Whitelaw 's abode . no sweet - scented woodbine or pale monthly rose beautify the front of the house in spring or summer time . the neglected ivy have overgrow one end of the long stone building and creep almost to the ponderous old chimney ; and this decoration , which have come of itself , be the only spot of greenery about the place . five tall poplar grow in a row about a hundred yard from the front window ; these , strange to say , Mr. Whitelaw have suffer to remain . they serve to add a little extra gloom to the settle grimness of the place , and perhaps harmonise with his taste . 

 within Wyncomb Farmhouse be no more attractive than without . the room be low and dark ; the window , make obscure by mean of heavy woodwork and common glass , let in what light they do admit with a grudging air , and seem to frown upon the inmate of the chamber they be suppose to beautify . there be all manner of gloomy passage , and unexpected flight of half - a - dozen stair or so , in queer angle of the house , and there be a prevail darkness everywhere ; for the Whitelaws of depart generation , object to the window tax , have block up every casement that it be possible to block up ; and the stranger explore Wyncomb Farmhouse be always come upon those blank plaster window , which have an unpleasant ghostly aspect , and set he long for a fireman 's hatchet to hew they open and let in the light of day . 

 the furniture be of the old , black with age , worm - eat , ponderous ; queer old four - post bedstead , with dingy hanging of greenish brown or yellowish green , from which every vestige of the original hue have fade long ago ; clumsy bureau , and stiff high - back chair with thick leg and gouty foot , heavy to move and uncomfortable to sit upon . the house be clean enough , and the bare floor of the numerous bed - chamber , which be only enliven here and there with small strip or band of dutch carpet , send up a homely odour of soft soap ; for Mrs. Tadman take a fierce delight in cleaning , and the solitary household drudge who toil under her order have a hard time of it . there be a dismal kind of neatness about everything , and a bleak empty look in the sparsely furnish room , which wear no pleasant sign of occupation , no look of home . the humblest cottage , with four tiny square room and a thatched roof , and just a patch of old - fashioned garden with a sweetbri hedge and rose grow here and there among the cabbage ; would have be a pleasanter habitation than Wyncomb , Ellen Carley think . 

 Mr. Whitelaw exhibit an unwonted liberality upon this occasion . the dinner be a ponderous banquet , and the dessert a noble display of nut and orange , fig and almond and raisin , flank by two old - fashioned decanter of port and sherry ; and both the bailiff and his host do ample justice to the feast . it be a long dreary afternoon of eat and drink ; and Ellen be not sorry to get away from the prim wainscote parlour , where her father and Mr. Whitelaw be solemnly sip their wine , to wander over the house with Mrs. Tadman . 

 it be about four o'clock when she slip quietly out of the room at that lady 's invitation , and the lobby and long passage have a shadowy look in the decline light . there be light enough for she to see the room , however ; for there be no rare collection of old china , no picture or adornment of any kind , to need a minute inspection . 

 " it be a fine old place , be not   it ? " ask Mrs. Tadman . " there be not many farmer can boast of such a house as Wyncomb . " 

 " it be large enough , " Ellen answer , with a tone which imply the reverse of admiration ; " but it be not a place I should like to live in . I be not one to believe in ghost or such nonsense , but if I could have any such foolish thought , I should have they here . the house look as if it be haunt , somehow . " 

 Mrs. Tadman laugh a shrill hard laugh , and rub her skinny hand with an air of satisfaction . 

 " you be not easy to please , Miss Carley , " she say ; " most folk think a deal of Wyncomb ; for , you see , it be only they that live in a house as can know how dull it be ; and as to the place be haunt , I never hear tell of anything of that kind . the Whitelaws be not   the kind of people to come back to this world , unless they come to fetch their money , and then they would come fast enough , I warrant . I use to see a good deal of my uncle , John Whitelaw , when I be a girl , and never do a son take after his father close than my cousin Stephen take after he ; just the same saving prudent way , and just the same masterful temper , always keep under in that quiet way of his . " 

 as Ellen Carley show herself profoundly indifferent to the light and shade of Mr. Whitelaw 's character , Mrs. Tadman do not pursue the subject , but with a gentle sigh lead the way to another room , and so on from room to room , till they have explore all that floor of the house . 

 " there be the attic above ; but you will not   care to see they , " she say . " the shepherd and five other man sleep up there . Stephen think it keep they steadier sleeping under the same roof with their master ; and he be able to ring they up of a morning , and to know when they go to their work . it be weary for I to have to get up and see to their breakfast , but I can not   trust Martha Holden to do that , or she would let they eat we out of house and home . there be no knowing what man like that can eat , and a side of bacon would go as fast as if you be to melt it down to tallow . but you must know what they be , Miss Carley , have to manage for your father . " 

 " yes , " Ellen answer , " I be use to hard work . " 

 " ah , " murmur the matron , with a sigh , " you would have plenty of it , if you come here . " 

 they be at the end of a long passage by this time ; a passage lead to the extreme end of the house , and form part of that ivy - cover wing which seem old than the rest of the building . it be on a low level than the other part , and they have descend two or three step at the entrance to this passage . the ceiling be low too , the beam that support they more massive , the diamond - pan window small and more heavily leaded , and there be a faint musty odour as of a place that be keep shut up and uninhabited . 

 " there be nothing more to see here , " say Mrs. Tadman quickly ; " I have well go back . I do not   know what bring I here ; it be talk , I suppose , make I come without think . there be nothing to show you this way . " 

 " but there be another room there , " Ellen say , point to a door just before they a heavy clumsily - make door , paint black . 

 " that room well , yes ; it be a kind of a room , but have not   be use for fifty year and more , I have   hear say . Stephen keep seed there and such - like . it be always lock , and he keep the key of it . " 

 there be nothing in this closed room to excite either curiosity or interest in Ellen 's mind , and she be turn away from the door with perfect indifference , when she start and suddenly seize Mrs. Tadman 's arm . 

 " Hark ! " she say , in a frightened , breathless way ; " do you hear that ? " 

 " what , child ? " 

 " do you say there be no one in there no one ? " 

 " Lord bless your heart , no , Miss Carley , nor ever be . what a turn you do give I , grasp hold of my arm like that ! " 

 " I hear something in there a footstep . it must be the servant . " 

 " what , Martha Holden ! I should like to see she venture into any room Stephen keep private to himself . besides , that door 's keep lock ; try it , and satisfy yourself . " 

 the door be indeed lock a door with a clumsy old - fashioned latch , securely fasten by a staple and padlock . Ellen try it with her own hand . 

 " be there no other door to the room ? " she ask . 

 " none ; and only one window , that look into the wood - yard , and be almost always block up with the wood pile outside it . you must have hear the muslin bag of seed blow about , if you hear anything . " 

 " I hear a footstep , " say Ellen firmly ; " a human footstep . I tell you the house be haunt , Mrs. Tadman . " 

 " Lor , Miss Carley , I wish you would not   say such thing ; it be enough to make one 's blood turn cold . do come downstairs and have a cup of tea . it be quite dark , I declare ; and you have   give I the shiver with your queer talk . "

 " it be the seed - bag , of course . " 

 " they could not   make a noise like human footstep . however , it be no business of mine , Mrs. Tadman , and I do not   want to frighten you . " 

 they go downstairs to the parlour , where the tea - tray and a pair of candle be soon bring , and where Mrs. Tadman stir the fire into a blaze with an indifference to the consumption of fuel which make her kinsman stare , even on that hospitable occasion . the blaze make the dark wainscote room cheerful of aspect , however , which the two candle could not have do , as their light be almost absorb by the gloomy panelling . 

 after tea there be whist again , and a considerable consumption of spirit - and - water on the part of the two gentleman , in which Mrs. Tadman join modestly , with many protestation , and , with the air of take only an occasional spoonful , contrive to empty her tumbler , and allow herself to be persuade to take another by the bailiff , whose joviality on the occasion be inexhaustible . 

 the day 's entertainment come to an end at last , to Ellen 's inexpressible relief ; and her father drive her home in the yellow gig at rather an alarming pace , and with some tendency towards heel over into a ditch . they get over the brief journey safely , however , and Mr. Carley be still in high good humour . he go off to see to the putting up of his horse himself , tell his daughter to wait till he come back , he have something particular to say to she before she go to bed . 

 CHAPTER XXXIII 

 " what must be shall be " 


 Ellen Carley wait in the little parlour , dimly light by one candle . the fire have very nearly go out , and she have some difficulty in brighten it a little . she wait very patiently , wonder what her father could have to say to she , and not anticipate much pleasure from the interview . he be go to talk about Stephen Whitelaw and his hateful money perhaps . but let he say what he would , she be prepared to hold she own firmly , determined to provoke he by no open opposition , unless matter come to an extremity , and then to let he see at once and for ever that her resolution be fix , and that it be useless to persecute she . 

 " if I have to go out of this house to - night , I will not flinch , " she say to herself . 

 she have some time to wait . it have be past midnight when they come home , and it be a quarter to one when William Carley come into the parlour . he be in an unusually communicative mood to - night , and have be superintend the grooming of his horse , and talk to the underling who have wait up to receive he . 

 he be a little unsteady in his gait as he come into the parlour , and Ellen know that he have drink a good deal at Wyncomb . it be no new thing for she to see he in this condition unhappily , and the shrink shudder sensation with which he inspire she to - night be painfully familiar . 

 " it be very late , father , " she say gently , as the bailiff fling himself heavily into an arm - chair by the fire - place . " if you do not   want I for anything particular , I should be glad to go to bed . " 

 " would you , my lass ? " he ask grimly . " but , you see , I do want you for something particular , something uncommon particular ; so there be no call for you to be in a hurry . sit down yonder , " he add , point to the chair opposite his own . " I have   get something to say to you , something serious . " 

 " Father , " say the girl , look he full in the face , pale to the lip , but very firm , " I do not   think you be in a state to talk seriously of anything . " 

 " o , you do not , do not   you , Miss Impudence ? you think I be drunk , perhaps . you will find that , drunk or sober , I have   only one mind about you , and that I mean to be obey . sit down , I tell you . I be not in the humour to stand any nonsense to - night . sit down . " 

 Ellen obey this mandate , utter with a fierceness unusual even in Mr. Carley , who be never a soft - speak man . she seat herself quietly on the opposite side of the hearth , while her father take down his pipe from the chimney - piece , and slowly fill it , with hand that tremble a little over the accustomed task . 

 when he have light the pipe , and smoke about half - a - dozen whiff with a great assumption of coolness , he address himself to his daughter in an altered and conciliate tone . 

 " well , Nelly , " he say , " you have   have a rare day at Wyncomb , and a regular ramble over the old house with Steph 's cousin . what do you think of it ? " 

 " I think it be a queer gloomy old place enough , father . I wonder there be any one can live in it . the dark bare - look room give I the horror . I use to think this house be dull , and seem as if it be haunt ; but it be lively and gay as can be , compare to Wyncomb . " 

 " Humph ! " mutter the bailiff . " you be a fanciful young lady , Miss Nell , and do not   know a fine substantial old house when you see one . life 's come a little too easy to you , perhaps . it might have be well for you if you have see more of the rough side . be your own missus too soon , and missus of such a place as this , have spoil you a bit . I tell you , Nell , there be not   a well house in Hampshire than Wyncomb , though it may not   suit your fanciful notion . do you know the size of Stephen Whitelaw 's farm ? " 

 " no , father ; I have   never think about it . " 

 " what do you say to three hundred acre over three hundred , nigh to four perhaps ? " 

 " I suppose it be a large farm , father . but I know nothing about such thing . " 

 " you suppose it be large , and you know nothing about such thing ! " cry the bailiff , with an air of supreme irritation . " I do not   believe any man be ever plague with such an aggravating daughter as mine . what do you say to be mistress of such a place , girl ? mistress of close upon four hundred acre of land ; not another man 's servant , bind to account for every blade of grass and every ear of corn , as I be , but free and independent mistress of the place , with the chance of be leave a widow by and by , and have it all under your own thumb ; what do you say to that ? " 

 " only the same that I have always say , father . nothing would ever persuade I to marry Stephen Whitelaw . I would rather starve . " 

 " and you shall starve , if you stick to that , " roar William Carley with a blasphemous oath . " but you will not   be such a fool , Nell . you will hear reason ; you will not   stand out against your poor old father and against your own interest . the long and the short of it be , I have   give Whitelaw my promise that you shall be his wife between this and Easter . " 

 " what ! " exclaim Ellen , with a faint cry of horror ; " you do not   mean that you have   promise that , father ! you can not   mean it ! " 

 " I can and do mean it , lass . " 

 " then you have   make a promise that will never be keep . you might have know as much when you make it . I be sure I have   be plain - speak enough about Stephen Whitelaw . " 

 " that be a girl 's silly talk . I do not   think to find you a fool when I come to the point . I let you have your say , and look to time to bring you to reason . Come , Nell , you be not go against your father , be you ? " 

 " I must , father , in this . I would rather die twenty death than marry that man . there be nothing I would not   rather do . " 

 " be not   there ? you would rather see your father in gaol , I suppose , if it come to that ? "

 DOUBTFUL INFORMATION

 CHAPTER XXXV 

 BOUGHT with a price 


 Ellen Carley be not allow any time to take back the promise give to her father , have she be inclined to do so .  CHAPTER xxxvi 

 come ROUND 


 Possessed with a thorough distrust of Mr. Medler and only half satisfied as to the fact of Marian 's safety , Gilbert Fenton lose no time in seek professional aid in the work of investigate this perplexing social mystery . he go once more to the metropolitan detective who have be with he in Hampshire , and whose labour there have prove so futile . the task now to be perform seem easy enough .  CHAPTER XXXVII 

 a full CONFESSION

 CHAPTER XXXVIII 

 an ILL - OMENED wedding

"

 February come to an end ; and when March once begin , there seem no pause or breathing - time for Ellen Carley till the 10th . and yet she have little business to occupy she during those bleak day of early spring . it be the horror of that rapid flight of time , which seem independent of her own life in its hideous swiftness . idle or busy , it be all the same . the day would not linger for she ; the dreaded 10th be close at hand .

Ellen have plead urgently that thing might be arrange as quietly as possible ; and the master of Wyncomb , who hat spend money , and who apprehend that the expense of any festivity would in all probability fall upon his own shoulder , be very well pleased to assent to this request of his betrothed .

"

 Mr. Whitelaw and Mrs. Tadman be at the church when the bailiff and his daughter arrive . the farmer have make a scarecrow of himself in a new suit of clothe , which he have order in honour of this important event , after a great deal of vacillation , and more than one countermand to the Malsham tailor who make the garment . at the last he be not quite clear in his mind as to whether he want the clothe , and the outlay be a serious one . Mr. Whitelaw 's chaise - cart be wait for they ; and they all four get in , and drive at once to Wyncomb ; where there be another ponderous dinner , very much like the banquet of new - year's - day , and where the bailiff drink freely , after his will not , and grow somewhat uproarious towards tea - time , though Mr. Whitelaw 's selection of port and sherry be not of a kind to tempt a connoisseur . 

 there be to be no honeymoon trip . Stephen Whitelaw do not understand the philosophy of run away from a comfortable home to spend money in furnish lodging ; and he have say as much , when the officious Tadman suggest a run to Weymouth , or Bournemouth , or a fortnight in the Isle of Wight . to Ellen it be all the same where the rest of her life should be spend . it could not be otherwise than wretched henceforward , and the scene of her misery matter nothing . so she utter no complaint because her husband bring she straight home to Wyncomb Farmhouse , and her wed life begin in that dreary dwelling - place . 

 CHAPTER XXXIX 

 A DOMESTIC mystery 


 it be near the end of March , but still bleak cold weather . Ellen Carley have be marry something less than a fortnight , and have come to look upon the dismal old farm - house by the river with a more accustomed eye than when Mrs. Tadman have take she from room to room on a journey of inspection . not that the place seem any less dreary and ugly to she to - day than it have seem at the very first . familiarity could not make it pleasant . she hat the house and everything about and around it , as she hat her husband , with a rooted aversion , not to be subdue by any endeavour which she might make now and then and she do honestly make such endeavour to arrive at a more christian - like frame of mind . 

 notwithstanding this deeply - seat instinctive dislike to all her surrounding , she endure her fate quietly , and do her duty with a patient spirit which might fairly be accept as an atonement for those inward rebellious feeling which she could not conquer . have submit to be the scapegoat of her father 's sin , she bear her burden very calmly , and fulfil the sacrifice without any outward mark of martyrdom . 

 she go about the work of the farm - house with a resolute active air that puzzle Mrs. Tadman , who have fully expect the young wife would play the fine lady , and leave all the drudgery of the household to she . but it really seem as if Ellen like hard work . she go from one task to another with an indefatigable industry , an energy that never give way . only when the day 's work in house and dairy be do do her depression of spirit become visible . then , indeed , when all be finish , and she sit down , neatly dress for the afternoon , in the parlour with Mrs. Tadman , it be easy to see how utterly hopeless and miserable this young wife be . the pale fix face , the listless hand clasp loosely in her lap , every attitude of the droop figure , betray the joyless spirit , the broken heart . at these time , when they be alone together , wait Stephen Whitelaw 's come home to tea , Mrs. Tadman 's heart , not entirely harden by long year of self - seeking , yearn towards her kinsman 's wife ; and the secret animosity with which she have at first regard she change to a silent pity , a compassion she would fain have express in some form or other , have she dare .

Ellen feel that the woman be kindly disposed towards she , and she be not ungrateful ; but her heart be quite shut against sympathy , her sorrow be too profound to be lighten ever so little by human friendship . it be a dull despair , a settle conviction that for her life could never have again a single charm , that her day must go on in their slow progress to the grave unlightene by one ray of sunshine , her burden carry to the end of the dreary journey unrelieve by one hour of respite . it seem very hard for one so young , not quite three - and - twenty yet , to turn she back upon every hope of happiness , to be oblige to say to herself , " for I the sun can never shine again , the world I live in can never more seem beautiful , or beautiful only in bitter contrast to my broken heart . " but Ellen tell herself that this fate be her , and that she must need face it with a resolute spirit . 

 the household work employ her mind in some measure , and keep she , more or less , from thinking ; and it be for this reason she work with such unflinche industry , just as she have work in the last month or two at the Grange , try to shut her eye to that hateful future which lie so close before she .  as to his wife 's feeling towards himself , whether those be cold indifference or hidden dislike , that be an abstruse and remote question which Mr. Whitelaw never take the trouble to ask himself . she be his wife . he have win she , that be the grand point ; whatever disinclination she might have feel for the alliance , whatever love she might have cherish for another , have be trample down and subjugate , and he , Stephen Whitelaw , have obtain the desire of his heart . he have win she , against that penniless young jackanape , lawyer Randall 's son , who have treat he with mark contempt on more than one occasion when they happen to come across each other in Malsham Corn - exchange , which be hold in the great covered quadrangular courtyard of the chief inn at Malsham , and be a popular lounge for the inhabitant of that town . he have win she ; her own sentiment upon the subject of this marriage be of very little consequence . he have never expect to be love by his wife , his own idea of that passion call love being of the vague ; but he mean to be obey by she . she have begin well , have take her new duty upon herself in a manner that gladden his sordid soul ; and although they have be marry nearly a fortnight , she have give no hint of a desire to know the extent of his wealth , or where he keep any little hoard of ready money that he might have by he in the house . nor on market - day have she express any wish to go with he to Malsham to spend money on drapery ; and he have an idea , sedulously cultivate by Mrs. Tadman , that young woman be perpetually want to spend money at draper ' shop . altogether , that first fortnight of his married life have be most satisfactory , and Mr. Whitelaw be inclined to regard matrimony as a wise and profitable institution . 

 the day 's work be do , and Ellen be sit with Mrs. Tadman in the every - day parlour , wait for the return of her lord and master from Malsham . it be not a market - day , but Stephen Whitelaw have announce at dinner - time that he have an appointment at Malsham , and have set out immediately after dinner in the chaise - cart , much to the wonderment of Mrs. Tadman , who be an inveterate gossip , and never easy until she arrive at the bottom of any small household mystery . she wonder not a little also at Ellen 's supreme indifference to her husband 's proceeding .

" I have not   know he go to Malsham , except of a market - day , not once in a twelvemonth . it must be a rare business to take he there in the middle of the week ; for he can not   abide to leave the farm in working - hour , except when he be right down oblige to it . nothing go on the same when his back 's turn , he say ; there be always something wrong . and if it be an appointment with any one belong to Malsham , why could not   it have stand over till Saturday ? it must be something out of the common that will not   keep a couple of day . I can not   make top nor tail of it . "

Mr. Whitelaw 's notion of tea be a solid meal , which leave he independent of the chance of supper , and yet open to do something in that way ; in case any light kickshaw , such as liver and bacon , a boil sheep 's head , or a beef - steak pie , should present itself to his notice .

 Ellen look at he , interested for the moment in spite of her apathetic indifference to all thing . the advent of a stranger be something so rare as to awaken a faint interest in the mind most dead to impression . she do not like his manner ; there be something false and hollow in his extreme politeness . and his face what be it in his face that startle she with such a sudden sense of strangeness and yet of familiarity ? 

 have she ever see he before ? yes ; surely that be the impression which send such a sudden shock through her nerve , which startle she from her indifference into eager wonder and perplexity . where have she see he before ? where and when ? long ago , or only very lately ? she could not tell . yet it seem to she that she have look at eye like those , not once , but many time in her life . and yet the man be utterly strange to she . that she could have see he before appear impossible . it must have be some one like he she have see , then . yes , that be it . it be the shadow of another face in his that have startle she with so strange a feeling , almost as if she have be look upon some ghostly thing . another face , like and yet unlike . 

 but what face ? whose face ?

" and so many speculation as there be now - a - day to tempt a man to his ruin railway and mine , and loan to Turks and Red Indians and such - like foreigner ; and Steph might so easy be tempt by the hope of large profit than he can make by farming . " there be no use in anticipate trouble . there may be nothing in this business after all .  CHAPTER XL 

 in PURSUIT

 CHAPTER XLI 

 OUTWARD BOUND 


 the bustle of departure be at its culminate point when John Saltram go on board the Oronoco , captain and officer scud hither and thither , give order and answer inquiry at every point , with a sharp , short , decisive air , as of command power in the last half - hour before a great battle ; steward and his underling ubiquitous ; passenger roam vaguely to and fro , in quest of nothing particular , and in a state of semi - distraction . 

 in this scene of confusion there be no one to answer Mr. Saltram 's eager inquire about those traveller whom he have pursue to this point . he do contrive , just about ten minute before the vessel sail , to capture the ubiquitous steward by the button - hole , and to ask for tiding of Mr. Nowell , before that excited functionary could wrench himself away . 

 " Mr. Nowell , sir ; upon my word , sir , I can not   say . yes , there be a gentleman of that name on board ; state - room number 5 and 7 ; get a daughter with he tall dark gentleman , with a moustache and beard . yes , sir , he be on deck just now , on the bridge ; but I do not   see he , I suppose he be go below . well look for he in the saloon , sir . "

 " Slander and lie be very strong , " John Saltram say to himself ; " but I do not think , when my dear love and I be once face to face , any power on earth can prevail against I . she must be change indeed , if it can ; she must be change indeed , if anything but a lie can part we . " 

 he have come on board the Oronoco prepare for the bad , and furnish with a slender outfit for the voyage , hurriedly purchase at a Liverpool clothier 's . he have plenty of money in his pocket enough to pay for his own and his wife 's return passage ; and the thought of this useless journey across the Atlantic trouble he very little . what do it matter where he be , if she be with he ? the mental torture he have undergo during all this time , in which he have seem in danger of lose she altogether , have teach he how dear she be how precious and perfect a treasure he have hold so lightly . 

 the vessel steamed put of the Mersey , and John Saltram , indifferent to the last glimpse of his native land , be still roam hither and thither , in quest of the familiar face he long with such a passionate yearn to see ; but up to this point he seek for his wife in vain .  " JOHN HOLBROOK . "

 indeed , there be need that thing should be well for John Saltram very speedily . he have set nature at defiance so far , act as if physical weakness be unknown to he . there be period in a man 's life in which nothing seem impossible to he ; in which by the mere force of will he triumph over impossibility . but such conquest be apt to be of the brief . John Saltram feel that he must very soon break down . the heavily throb heart , the ache limb , the dizzy sight , and parch throat , tell he how much this desperate chase have cost he . if he have strength enough to clasp his wife 's hand , to give her love greeting and tell she that he be true , it would be about as much as he could hope to achieve ; and then he feel that he would be glad to crawl into any corner of the vessel where he might find rest .
